ANNUAL REPORT for the YEAR 1904, on The Sanitary Condition and Vital Statistics of the Metropolitan Borough of Poplar, comprising the Parishes of ALL SAINTS, POPLAR, BROMLEY ST. LEONARD, and ST. MARY STRATFORD-LE-BOW, by FREDK. WM. ALEXANDER, Member of the Royal College of Surgeons of England, and Licentiate of the Royal College of Physicians of Edinburgh; Diplomatt in Public Health, Royal College of Physicians and Surgeons, England; Fellow and Member of the Council of the Royal Institute of Public Health, Member of the Lister Institute of Preventive Medicine: MEDICAL OFFICER OF HEALTH. Comprising the Parishes of All Saints, Poplar, Bromley St. Leonard, and St. Mary, Stratford-le-Bow. ANNUAL REPORT of the MEDICAL OFFICER OF HEALTH. To the Mayor, Aldermen and Councillors of the Metropolitan Borough of Poplar. Sir and Gentlemen, In accordance with "The Sanitary Officer's (London) Order, 1891," of the Local Government Board, dated 8th December, 1891, I have the pleasure of submitting to you my twelfth Annual Report, which Report deals with the Year 1904, and includes the Annual Reports of the Sanitary Inspectors, also the Report of the Lady Inspector and that of the Assistant Sanitary Inspector engaged in enforcing the bye-laws for Houses let in Lodgings or occupied by members of more than one family. VITAL STATISTICS. The population of the Borough, estimated to the middle of the year 1904, on the theory of the basis of the rate of chunge between the censuses of 1896 and 1901 being maintained, is 169,519 :— Registration London. Poplar. Bromley. Bow. 4,648,950 59,378 67,696 42,445 2 being 62.1 (see footnote) inhabitants per acre in Registration London, 72.84 per acre for the Borough of Poplar, 50.92 per acre in Poplar Parish, 111.8 in Bromley, and 76.47 in Bow.* These estimated populations are the figures used in calculating the various rates in the present Report for the year 1904. The various rates quoted in this Report for the years 1893 to 1900 are the rates as stated in previous Reports.; they are calculated upon the then estimated populations, and as far as possible from past Annual Reports for the Parish of Bow figures respecting certain diseases have been extracted and the rates worked out so that the three parishes might be compared, but the rates in Tables IY., V., and VI., have been calculated upon revised estimated populations based upon the known censuses of 1891, 1896, and 1901. In Bromley there were 43 illegitimate births, 18 males and 25 females. In Bow there were 7 illegitimate births, 3 males and 4 females, making a total of 118 in the Borough. The annual marriage rate per 1000 living being: — Poplar and Bromley. Bow. Borough of Poplar. County or London. 1894 14.07 — — — 1895 13.7 — — — 1896 16.0 — — — 1897 16.92 — — — 1898 17.92 — — — 1899 17.90 — — — 1900 18.36 — — — 1901 18.7 23.6 19.9 17.6 1902 14.3 21.0 15.9 17.8 1903 16.6 19.9 17.4 17.4 1904 16.9 19.6 17.6 17.0 a 2 4 In the 52 weeks 3264 deaths wore registered in the Borough of Poplar, 829 deaths having occurred in the Parish of Poplar, 1887 in Bromley, and 548 in Bow; from the 829 deaths which took place in Poplar there must be deducted 57 deaths of non-residents, and to the remainder must be added 342 deaths which occurred in outlying institutions, making a total of 1114 deaths of Poplar parishioners. Treating the 1887 registered deaths in Bromley in the same manner, viz., deducting 814 deaths of non-parishioners and adding 204 deaths in outlying institutions, the deaths to be assigned to this parish will be found to be 1277. From the 548 Bow deaths, there must be deducted 24 non-residents and added 239 deaths in outlying institutions, making a total of 763 deaths. In dealing separately with the parishes as above, it will be seen by referring to Tables I., II. and III. that in considering the deaths of non-residents and those from outlying institutions, that certain additions and deductions from the parishes have to be made in respect of themselves, but when considering the Borough as a whole, then from the 3264 deaths which were registered there must be deducted 522 deaths of actual non-residents and 412 deaths in institutions entirely outlying must be added, making a total for the Borough of 3154, upon which the death rate is calculated. The 1114 deaths in Poplar included 9 illegitimate children, 4 male and 5 female; the 1277 deaths in Bromley included 25 illegitimate children, 16 male and 9 female; the 763 Bow deaths included 8 illegitimate children, 2 male and 6 female, making a total for the Borough of 42 deaths of illegitimate children. See Table XIII., page 24, East Districts. The Deptford Borough Council had under consideration the suggestion that the duties of the Guardians in respect of the registration of births and deaths might with advantage be transferred to the Borough Councils and the registrations effected at Municipal buildings, instead of at the private residences of the Registrars, and they asked for the opinion of the Council on the matter. The Committee concurred in the suggestion, and recommended the Council accordingly. Borough of Poplar. 1893 96 97 73 266 1894 96 101 76 273 1895 114 100 70 284 1896 112 113 90 315 1897 123 140 96 359 1898 135 111 106 352 1899 141 136 131 408 1900 115 127 80 322 1901 125 97 80 302 1902 116 106 94 316 1903 118 113 84 315 1904 125 109 83 317 VENEREAL DISEASE. A copy was received, of a resolution moved by Sir J. Crichton Browne and adopted at a meeting presided over by the Bishop of Stepney, to the following effect, viz.:— " That this Meeting is of opinion that the Diseases, physical and " mental, resulting directly or by inheritance, from Venereal " Disease are so serious and numerous that it is desirable that " there should be full enquiry into the matter; particularly in the " direction whether there is adequate provision for the treatment " of the poorer classes in the various stages of the disease, and " urges that the public bodies connected with the health of the " people should take action in this matter." 11 It was suggested the Council should support an appeal to the Government for an enquiry as suggested. It was pointed out the facilities for treatment of in patients are quite inadequate, and could be probably largely developed in existing hospitals if the desirability of such development could be shown. The Committee recommended the Council to support an appeal to the Government for an enquiry as proposed. TABLE I. Totals 525 13 177 239 25 66 5 286 * See page 78. 32 ZYMOTIC DISEASES Notifications, Hospital Accommodation, Infectious diseases in Milk Shops and on Outworkers' premises. There were 603 notifications of infectious diseases in Poplar, 951 in Bromley, and 525 in Bow, making a total of 2,079 for the Borough of Poplar; these do not include 92 second notifications. In the above figures, no deductions have been made for cases removed to the Metropolitan Asylums Board's hospitals, which were certified as not having a notifiable disease. Of the 603 patients notified in Poplar, 264 were removed to hospitals; of the 951 in Bromley, 460 were isolated, and of the 525 in Bow 286 were removed, so that of a total of 2079 notifiable diseases in the Borough 1010 patients had been removed from their homes. Respecting the removal of patients to the Hospitals of the Metropolitan Asylums Board the following letter, dated the 1st of September, was received from the Clerk of that Authority:— "Representations having been made to the Managers as to the difficulties experienced in obtaining the removal of patients to hospital after this office is closed at 8 p.m., I have now to inform you that, as an experiment, the Managers have decided to keep the Ambulance Department open daily until midnight for six months from the 5th instant. 1 shall be glad if you will communicate this information to all the Officers of your Council concerned," c 34 In giving the above numbers of notifications, no deductions have been made for the following cases, which it will be seen developed other diseases, either shortly after admission or after being in the hospital some time, whilst the list also shows that some patients removed to the Metropolitan Asylums Board's hospitals, after being detained in those institutions some days were returned home certified as not suffering from the complaint or any other notifiable disease. The corrected number of notifications would be — Poplar 603 — 5 = 598 or 10.07 per 1000 living. Bromley 951 — 29 = 922 or 13.61 „ ,, ,, Bow 525 — 11 = 514 or 12.10 ,, ,, ,, Borough of Poplar 2079 — 45 = 2034 or 11.99 ,, ,, ,, In the course of the year 1904 it will be seen no fewer than 54 patients or a percentage on the total admissions of 5.5 were, after admission at the fever hospitals, found not to be suffering from the diseases mentioned in the medical certificates upon which they were removed to hospital. The percentage on the total scarlet fever cases was 5.0, diphtheria cases 4.9, and enteric fever cases 17.6. Amongst the 20 cases wrongly certified as scarlet fever there were 9 of measles, 1 of rotheln, and 1 of tubercular meningitis. Amongst the 24 cases wrongly certified as diphtheria were one of measles, 2 of stomatitis, and 2 of retro-pharyngeal abscess. Amongst the 9 cases wrongly certified as enteric fever were 1 of measles, 2 of pneumonia, 1 of lumbago, 1 of malignant-endocarditis, 1 of tuberculosis of lungs, 1 of infantile diarrhoea, and 1 of tonsillitis. In the course of the year 1903 no fewer than 1,913 patients against 2,108 in 1902 or a percentage on the total admissions of 37 10.4 in 1903 and 8.6 in 1902 were, after admission at the fever hospitals, found not to be suffering from the diseases mentioned in the medical certificates upon which they were removed to hospital. The number of cases wrongly notified in Poplar and London for the years 1899—1904, and removed to Infectious Diseases Hospitals have been as follows : — Percentage of mistakes in total admissions. Poplar. London. 1899 49 cases 8.4 6.3 1900 60 „ 7.3 7.9 1901 131 „ 13.1 9.2 1902 93 „ 6.3 8.6 1903 46 „ 6.5 10.4 1904 54 „ 5.5 Facilities are still afforded to practitioners to have swabbings from throats of doubtful cases of Diphtheria, and specimens of blood from doubtful cases of Typhoid Fever bacteriologically examined. In every case of wrongful notification the Public Health and Housing Committee requires an explanation from the Practitioner concerned, and where this is not satisfactory the facts are reported to the Council. The Committee fully concur with the Metropolitan Asylums Board that "the introduction into the Board's Hospitals of cases of other diseases than those which such hospitals are designed to treat" should be prevented as far as practicable. Arrangements are made that the positive results of bacteriological examinations are communicated to the officers of the Metropolitan Asylums Board at the time when the removals of patients are telephoned. One case of erysipelas and one of measles were connected with milkshops in Poplar. One case of diphtheria, one of small pox, two of chicken pox, two of scarlet fever, three of measles and two of erysipelas were connected with milkshops in Bromley. One case of scarlet fever and four of measles were connected with like premises in Bow. The sale of milk was stopped until after the premises and utensils had been disinfected. 38 For notices which had been received from Port Sanitary Authorities respecting passengers coming to the Borough of Poplar see page 54. Nineteen premises with outworkers were connected with infectious cases notified, 6 in Poplar, 6 in Bromley, and 7 in Bow. One small pox, 5 scarlet fever, 8 diphtheria, 1 typhoid fever, 1 erysipelas, and three chicken pox. No notices were served under the Factory and Workshop Act, 1901, Section 110, but, where there was any risk of infection the work was discontinued, and any work found on the premises was disinfected before being returned. Eight cases of measles were also connected with outworkers' premises, and where work was found on the premises, it was disinfected before being returned if there were any likelihood of infection, but there is no power to prevent work being executed on outworkers' premises where measles exists. See page 87. The Public Health and Housing Committee considered a communication from a Eelief Committee of the Poplar Guardians complaining that no maintenance allowance was granted to a family on the withholding by the Medical Officer of Health of a clearance certificate after a case of infectious disease, to a person engaged on homework. The Eelief Committee was informed the Council has no power to grant relief as suggested. Under Section 110 (1) of the Factory and Workshop Act, 1901, if any inmate of a house is suffering from an infectious disease, the Council may make an order forbidding homo work to be given out to any person living or working in that house. The Committee were of opinion that when it is necessary, in the interests of public safety, for such action to be taken, the Council should have discretionary power to award compensation in any case, and recommended the Council that the Local Government Board be asked to promote legislation in the direction indicated. Pending the reply of the Local Government Board to the request of the Council to promote legislation giving Sanitary Authorities discretionary powers to award compensation to inmates of a house 30 or laundry who have lost their home or laundry work through a case of infectious disease on the premises, the Committee considered the maintenance of such persons should be left to the discretion of the Medical Officer of Health in each case, and the Committee recommended the Council— " That the maintenance of the inmates of a house who have " lost their home or laundry work through an infectious " disease existing or having existed on the premises, should " be left to the discretion of the Medical Officer of Health: " maintenance to be either in money or kind. The Medical " Officer to report to the Public Health Committee at their " next meeting the action he has taken in any case, and " application to be made to the Local Government Board for " relief under the Local Authorities Expenses Act in respect " of any expenditure incurred." The Committee considered a communication from the Incorporated Guild of Hairdressers, which Guild, in conjunction with the City Corporation, had arranged a set of regulations for the conduct of the business of a barber and hairdresser, with a view to the prevention of the spread of contagious diseases through improper methods in barbers' shops. It had been arranged that any hairdresser applying to the Guild for a certificate should be furnished with a form of certificate of suitability of his premises, to be signed by the Medical Officer of the City of London, after inspection, and subject to this, and compliance with the regulations approved, the Guild certificate would be granted. It was suggested by the Guild that similar arrangements should be made with the Borough Councils for inspection and certification in their respective Boroughs by the Medical Officers. The Committee were of opinion such a course is desirable in the interests of public health, and recommended the Council— "That the Medical Officer of Health be authorised to give "certificates of suitability of premises to applicants for the " Hairdressers' Guild Certificates." 40 ALPHABETICAL STREET LIST OF INFECTIOUS DISEASES. The attention of the London County Council was called to the spread of small-pox through the agency of vagrants, and it was suggested the Council should convene a Conference of the Sanitary Authorities of England and Wales to consider the question. The London County Council agreed to convene the Conference, and on the 10th November at the County Hall, Spring Gardens, together with Mr. Councillor Bacon, I attended a Conference on the "Spread of Infectious Disease by Vagrants." The following are the Resolutions adopted by the Conference. GENERAL MEASURES FOR THE PREVENTION OF THE SPREAD OF INFECTIOUS DISEASE BY VAGRANTS. (1) (a) That this Conference of urban and sanitary authorities of Kngland and Wales recognises the increasing amount of habituai D 50 vagrancy as the cause of wide-spread and disastrous consequences to the public health, and is of opinion that much more effective measures than are at present adopted should be taken for preventing the spread of infectious disease by vagrants, and for effectually dealing with this great and growing danger. (b) That the evil can only be met by conferring further powers upon the local authorities, viz., the sanitary authority, the board of guardians, and the magistracy. (2) That means should be provided for the detention and isolation of any vagrants found wandering in a public place, if reasonably suspected of being liable to convey infectious disease. (3) That this Conference is of opinion that it is desirable that (a) Parliamentary powers should be sought for the compulsory vaccination and re-vaccination of all vagrants unable to produce proof of being sufficiently protected against small-pox on entering casual wards or common lodging houses, who, in the opinion of the sanitary authority, have been exposed to the infection of smallpox, and also that sanitary authorities should have power to grant such compensation as they think necessary to persons vaccinated or re-vaccinated at their request who may be prevented on that account from work; (b) It should be an offence to withhold information or make false statements to the sanitary authority in carrying out its powers with respect to the disease. (4) That in order to deal effectually with the transmission of notifiable infectious disease by tramps, power should be given to county councils to enforce the existing laws in all cases where sanitary authorities in the county refuse or neglect to do so. • (5) That the Local Government Board should obtain power to secure weekly reurns of all cases of infectious disease from all central authorities throughout the country, and circulate the same. 51 (6) That it is desirable that in districts comprising groups of counties and county boroughs, intelligence bureaux should be established, to which information should be sent from sanitary authorities and workhouses in the district, of persons of the wandering class who have been exposed to the infection of smallpox; and that a printed copy of such information should be distributed from the bureau to every sanitary authority and board of guardians in the district, and that the expense of working the bureau should be met by contributions from the county councils and county boroughs forming the district. (7) That this Conference is of opinion that the port sanitary authorities should be authorised to take more stringent precautions to prevent the importation of disease by persons arriving by vessel at the ports who are suffering from small-pox, or who have been exposed to infection by that disease. COMMON LODGING-HOUSES AND CASUAL WARDS. (8) That the local authority should have increased control over common lodging-houses, their keepers and occupants. Thus the Local Authority should have power— (a) To medically examine the inmates. (6) To detain and isolate persons exposed to infection and to disinfect them and their clothes. (c) To temporarily close a common lodging-house in whole or in part, compensation to be given to the keeper of the house. (9) That the local authority should have power to order the keeper of a common lodging-house in which there has been infectious disease to refuse fresh admissions for such time as may be required by the authority. (10) That the local authority should be empowered to require medical examination and disinfection of all persons entering casual wards. D 2 52 (11) That the local sanitary authority should have power to require the removal and isolation of any inmate of a casual ward who may reasonably be suspected of being liable to convey infectious disease. (12) That the local authority should have full power to require the cleansing of the person and the disinfection of the clothes of any person in a casual ward, whether infected or exposed to infection. LABOUR BUREAUX. (13) (a) That the time has arrived when the Local Government Board should promote legislation for the establishment of labour bureaux in the areas of every county council and every county borough council. (b) That this Conference is also of opinion that it is desirable that a national voluntary agency should be formed for assisting bona-fide working men while travelling through the country in search of work, and that such agency should be worked on the same lines as the Inter-Cantonal Union of Switzerland for the relief of poor travellers. (14) That the unemployed travelling buna-fide in search of work, not being habitual vagrants, should not be treated as vagrants, but as far as possible be assisted to obtain employment. LABOUR COLONIES. (15) That the time has arrived when the Local Government Board should promote legislation for the establishment of labour colonies for the compulsory detention of habitual vagrants until they have acquired power to work and self restraint. (16) That this Conference approves and affirms the resolutions passed by the executive council of the Association of Poor Law Unions in England and Wales at their meeting in September, 1903, as follows: — (a) That boards of guardians should be grouped in suitable areas to provide labour colonies for vagrants. 53 (b) That such colonies should be established and governed by the joint committees of the boards of guardians of the unions in the said areas. (c) That habitual vagrants should be sent to such labour colonies by magistrates. (d) That the expenses of each colony should be spread over the unions in the areas served by such colonies. CHILDREN OF VAGRANTS. (17) That the powers of the poor law guardians under the Poor Law Acts of 1889 and 1899 to assume and exercise, in certain circumstances, parental rights over the children of pauper parents, should be extended so as to confer similar rights over the children of habitual vagrants. GENERALLY. (18) That, in order to give practical effect to the foregoing resolutions, this Conference is in favour of uniting with the County Councils Association, the Association of Municipal Corporations, the Association of Metropolitan Borough Councils, the Magistracy, and the Association of Poor Law Unions of England' and Wales for the following purposes, viz.:—(a) To enlist the sympathy and cooperation of members of Parliament; (b) to appoint representatives to wait on the Local Government Board; (c) to give evidence before the Inter-Departmental Committee of the Government on the Vagrancy Question; (d) to take such other measures as may be thought desirable in support of the resolutions arrived at by the Conference. That a Committee be appointed to give effect to this resolution. DISINFECTING DEPARTMENT. The following figures give the number of rooms and articles disinfected: — 54 District. Number of Rooms. NOTICES PORT SANITARY AUTHORITIES. Three notices were received from the Commandant, Discharge Depot, Gosport, of men disembarked at Southampton from s.s. " Soudan" on which ship a case of small-pox was landed at Malta. One man arrived and was in good health, another man who was 55 coming to the Parish of Poplar became sick and was admitted into the Netley Hospital; the third man gave an address which could not be found, and the Commandant, Discharge Depot, Gosport, was notified of the fact. Notice was received on the 10th March from the Southampton Sanitary Authority of a passenger proceeding to the Poplar district from the s.s. " Assaye." The Commandant, Discharge Depot, Gosport, also forwarded notices on the 11th and 12th March respecting five persons from the same ship, and through the Medical Officer of the London County Council on the 15th March notice was received of another person, making seven persons in all. A case of small-pox had occurred on board. Four persons arrived and were kept under observation. Notice was received on the 30th March from the Port of London Sanitary Authority respecting five passengers from the s.s. " Minneapolis," from New York. A case of small-pox had occurred on board ten days previously to the vessel arrived in London on the 28th March. Four of the addresses belonged to other Boroughs, and the fifth address given was a non-existent number in the Poplar Borough. Notices were received on the 31st March from the Southampton Sanitary Authority of two passengers from H.M.T. "Plassy." Plague and small-pox had occurred on board. One passenger arrived, the other was stated to have remained on the ship. Notice was received on the 7th April from the Southampton Sanitary Authority of a passenger from H.M.T. "Sicilia," A case of small-pox had occurred on board. The passenger was not known at the address given. Notice was received on the 11th April from the Port of London Sanitary Authority of three passengers from s.s. "Macedonia" from Bombay. A case of small-pox was landed from the vessel at Marseilles on the 2nd April. Two of the passengers came to the Poplar Borough and one went to West Ham. 56 Notice was received from the Port of London Sanitary Authority of a steward from the s.s. "Moldavia" coming into the Poplar District. On arrival of the vessel in London six of the crew were removed suffering from scarlet fever. Pursuant to the Cholera Regulations, notice was received from the Hull and Goole Port Sanitary Authority of two persons coming to the Poplar District out of the s.s. " Agnes " from Bombay. Notice was received from the Medical Officer of the Port of London respecting two passengers proceeding to the Poplar District from s.s. "Malta" from Bombay. Two cases suspicious of plague were removed to the Port Sanitary Hospital from the vessel on arrival at Gravesend. The passengers were kept under observation. A notice was received from the Hull and Goole Port Sanitary Authority of six passengers coming from the s.s. "Comrie Castle" from Calcutta. One passenger belonged to West Ham, one to Stepney, and two to Poplar; one residence given in Poplar was an empty house and one address could not be located. The usual information was forwarded. Notice was received from the Port of London Sanitary Authority that one of the crew was proceeding to the Poplar Borough out of the s.s. "Weybridge" from Rosaria; a case of plague had been removed from the vessel at Gravesend. The man was kept under observation. Further notices were received from the Port of London Sanitary Authority, respecting men working on the s.s. "Weybridge" whilst in dock; 77 names and addresses were sent. One address given was in the Stepney Borough, and the information was forwarded on. One person was known at the address stated but did not arrive there. In eleven instances the persons could not be found at the addresses forwarded. The Port Sanitary Authority was communicated with, and four corrected addresses were furnished, one person was found to be living in 57 Stepney and the remaining six were not traced in the Poplar Borough. Through the Medical Officer of the London County Council, information was received respecting a person proceeding to the Poplar Borough out of the s.s. "Claverdon" from Rio-de-Janeiro. At the address given the person was not known. PUERPERAL FEVER. Fifteen cases of puerperal fever were notified, nine Poplar, four Bromley, and two Bow. Four cases were treated in the Sick Asylum, two in the London Hospital and one at St. Bartholomew's Hospital. Of the four cases treated in the Sick Asylum one case was notified as occurring in that institution, one in the Poplar Union Workhouse, and the other two were admitted from the Parish of Poplar. The two London Hospital cases were admitted from Poplar Parish, and the St. Bartholomew's case from the Parish of Bromley. Five deaths occurred from puerperal fever. Three Poplar, one Bromley and one Bow. Upon receipt of a notification of puerperal fever, the medical attendant is forthwith communicated with, requesting him to warn the attendants and midwife (if there be one), as to the dangerous infectious nature of the disease. The midwife (if there be one) and the attendants are immediately interviewed, and written to, warning them neither to attend nor go near any confinement case until some weeks have elapsed, and then only after they have had their clothes disinfected, which will be carried out by the Sanitary Authority for the district in which they reside. Should the midwife (if there be one), and the attendants reside out of the Borough, the facts are made known to the Medical Officer of Health of the district in which they reside. I he Committee had brought under their notice the matter of a nurse who had been nursing a case of puerperal fever, and had 58 been advised she was not to attend another confinement for some weeks after she had ceased nursing the patient, and was thereby debarred from obtaining her livelihood. The Committee were of opinion the subject of compensating such cases as this, as well as any midwife who may be suspended by the rules of the Central Midwives' Board, made under Section 8 (3) of the Midwives Act, 1902, should be brought under the notice of the Central Midwives' Board, and legislation promoted to enable compensation to be given by the Local Supervising Authorities. The Committee recommended the Council to endorse their opinion, and communications on the subject be addressed to the Central Midwives' Board and the London County Council. SMALL-POX. Of the 42 cases of small-pox notified within the Borough, 41 patients had the disease, as one case was returned home not having the complaint. One case notified to the Borough of Stepney was removed from the Stepney Union Workhouse. (1) Park Place, female, aged 49 years. The patient had been vaccinated in infancy, two good marks; and another very small one. The case was one of discrete small pox. There was no account as to how the patient had contracted the disease, she stated she had not been out of the house since Christmas. The patient was taken ill on the 22nd January, notified on the 28th January, and removed to the Small Pox Hospital same day. The inmates of the house consisted of husband and nine children, all of whom went to the Council's shelter, Glaucus Street, were bathed and their clothes disinfected, and remained at shelter until the house throughout was disinfected, cleansed, stripped and limewhitened. The father wished to have himself and his children re-vaccinated. 59 The Medical Officer of Health of the district where two of the daughters (contacts) worked was notified. The patient was an out-worker and work was found upon the premises at the time she was removed. The work was taken to the disinfecting station, Glaucus Street, the firm notified, and also the Medical Officer of Health of the district. (2) A coloured man suffering from small-pox walked into the Poplar Hospital on the 18th February and could give no proper account of himself except his name. He stated he had been sleeping with other coloured men in a lodging-house near a church in Poplar. The ambulance nurse, before taking the patient to the South Wharf, endeavoured to find out his address by calling at several lodging-houses, but the patient was not known. As soon as the authorities at Poplar Hospital telephoned to the Public Health Department that the case had been removed, the chief disinfectoir commenced calling at the Seaman's lodginghouses and Common lodging-houses. The London County Council was communicated with so that the inspectors of the Common lodging-houses might make enquiries. The Medical Officer of the South Wharf was telephoned to and asked to put further questions to the patient (who was evidently a sailor) as to the name of his ship and where he came from. The patient did not know the name of his ship, but he stated he had come from Newport News. The Medical Officer of the Port of London was immediately telephoned to and asked to make enquiries as to all ships which had arrived from Newport News. On the 19th February, the Port of London Medical Officer telephoned that the patient, with two other coloured men and nine other men, all cattlemen, had left the s.s. "Austriana" in Victoria Docks on the 16th February, but he did not know where they had gone to. GO In the meantime, the Sanitary Inspector, Mr. Johnson, and the Chief Disinfector, were making enquiries at the Seamen's and Common lodging-houses, and Mr. Johnson found that the patient had slept at a Common lodging-house in High Street, Poplar, with the two coloured men on the nights of the 16th and 17th of February. The Inspectors of the London County Council found some of the other contacts in Common lodging-houses in Whitechapel. The two coloured men were re-vaccinated and taken to the Council's Shelter, Glaucus Street, to be bathed and their clothes disinfected. The men spent the night at the shelter. The bed and bedding from three rooms at the Common lodginghouse were removed to the disinfecting apparatus and the three rooms were disinfected and limewashed. (3) Female, aged 16 years, Suffolk Street, removed 3rd March, stated to have been vaccinated in infancy. The patient during the day was a servant in Burdett Road, and slept at night at Alton Street, but when taken ill was sent home to her parents in Suffolk Street. Patient was an "out-patient" at Poplar Hospital at the same time as the negro from the s.s. "Austriana" was waiting to be seen. Refused re-vaccination at the time his six brothers and sisters and uncle were vaccinated or re-vaccinated. The usual precautions were taken in all the above cases. The contacts were notified by telephone and afterwards by letter to the Medical Officers of the districts in which they lived or gained their livelihood, and if contacts worked in the Poplar district, the firms for whom they were engaged were notified, and requested not to allow them to remain at work until they had had a bath and their clothes disinfected. The rooms from which the patients were removed were disinfected with formalin, stripped of their paper (if any) and then either washed down or limewhitened. Enquiries were made in all instances as to who executed the laundry work. E 2 68 The Public Vaccinator and the Vaccination Officer were also notified, and in many instances re-vaccination was carried out. In the case at the Common lodging-house, the Medical Officer of the London County Council was immediately notified of the fact. As far as possible contacts were kept under observation. With such precautions and active steps there is no doubt the repetition of the out-break of the disease such as there was in the years 1901 and 1902 was prevented. It will be seen that three of the above cases died, Nos. 15, 25, and 36. The Committee considered a reference from the Council on the subject of the maintenance in their own homes of small pox contacts. It was pointed out to the Council that the Local Government Board would be prepared to sanction maintenance of contacts in their own homes only in cases in which the Medical Officer advised that it was essential. The Committee were of opinion the matter should be left to the discretion of the Medical Officer of Health in each case, and recommended the Council: "That the maintenance of small-pox contacts in their own homes be left to the absolute discretion of the Medical Officer of Health." A man, aged 40 years, of Teviot Street, was notified as suffering from small-pox. He was removed to the South Wharf on the 24th March, and detained there under observation until the 31st March, when he was returned home as not suffering from that complaint, or any other notifiable disease. NOTIFICATION OF CHICKEN POX AND DIAGNOSIS OF DOUBTFUL CASES OF SMALL POX. The London County Council resolved and ordered that section 55 of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, with respect to the notification of infectious diseases should apply in the 69 tive County of London, for a period of four calendar months from the 8th April, to the disease known as chicken-pox. The Local Government Board approved the resolution of the London County Council as a case of emergency. The Local Government Board also approved the resolution of the London County Council extending the period for notification of chicken-pox for a further period of three months expiring on 7th November. With a view to facilitating the diagnosis of doubtful cases of small-pox occurring in the Administrative County of London, the Council made an arrangement under which, without fee, the services could be obtained of Dr. S. Bingham, formerly Medical Superintendent of one of the small-pox hospitals of the Metropolitan Asylums Board, or Dr. W. MacWanklyn, late small-pox referee to the Metropolitan Asylums Board, and Medical Superintendent of the River Ambulance Service. This arrangement continued in force until 7th August, 1904, but the sen-ices of Dr. MacWanklyn were retained until the 7th November. SHELTER. During the year 159 persons passed through the Council's Shelter in Yeo Street. The attention of the Public Health Committee was called to the Lower Shelter, which was not at all suitable or adequate to meet with the pressure of work. The Lower Shelter was the house of the Caretaker of the Glaucus Street Depot, and consists of two bedrooms, a caretaker's living-room and bedroom (one room), and a waiting-room, which was used for the contacts to have their meals in, a scullery and a bath-room. The great objection to the Lower Shelter, was, that those persons who had had their bath, and their clothes disinfected, came in 70 contact with those persons who were waiting for a bath and had not had their clothes disinfected, hence, there might have been re-infection. It was reported to the Committee that when the Upper Shelter, which consists of workshops, was given over by the Works Department, it was so arranged that the infected and disinfected contacts did not meet one another. Certain portions of what was used as the Upper Shelter had again been taken over by the Works Department, and, although these workshops might again be given up to the Public Health Department, it was pointed out to the Public Health Committee that to rely upon such an arrangement is not at all a proper state of affairs. At the time of the report there were two rooms in what was the Upper Shelter, divided by a workshop, a store room in which the Public Health Department keeps the necessary articles for stripping and cleansing rooms, and a bath room. The Committee referred the question of provision of adequate shelter accommodation at the Depot to the Borough Surveyor and myself for report. The Surveyor stated that the Upper Shelter, which was given over at one time by the Works Department to the Public Health Department, and was being used at the time of the outbreak as a workshop was still required by the Works Department, so that it appeared that if the house which was being used, and was once the Caretaker's house, was still to be used as a Shelter, structural alterations must have been carried out, and more room afforded; provision would also have to be made for the infected and disinfected contacts not to meet one another. It is also essential in a shelter that at least two waiting rooms should be provided, to prevent infected contacts of small-pox cases mixing up with contacts of other diseases. Another bath would have to be fitted up. 71 The Surveyor stated the present structure would not allow of extensive alterations, but as it was absolutely necessary, for the well-being of the community at large, the Borough should have a proper shelter, the Committee directed the Works Committee be asked to place the workshops at their disposal during the outbreak. The Works Committee agreed to the necessary temporary accommodation being afforded at Bromley Depot to meet the necessities of the outbreak. In the near future a permanent new shelter with disinfecting station will be erected on the land acquired by the Council in Glaucus Street. See "Mortuaries" page 91. VACCINATION. Borough of Poplar. 1893 Cases notified 627 854 313 1794 Deaths 39 48 13 100 1891 Cases notified 239 331 225 795 Deaths 12 22 8 42 73 Poplar. Bromley. Bow. Borough of Poplar. 1895 Cases notified 343 330 329 1002 Deaths 20 19 8 47 1896 Cases notified 274 429 243 946 Deaths 8 19 7 34 1897 Cases notified 251 476 373 1100 Deaths 6 12 . 10 28 1898 Cases notified 207 325 106 638 Deaths 3 13 6 22 1899 Cases notified 142 179 102 423 Deaths 2 4 3 9 1900 Cases notified 246 168 85 499 Death 3 3 1 7 1901 Cases notified 236 236 87 559 Deaths 5 5 4 14 1902 Cases notified 182 341 150 67J Deaths 5 11 6 21 1903 Cases notified 178 177 94 449 Deaths 8 5 7 20 1904 Cases notified 162 271 116 549 Deaths 10 12 2 24 On account of the alleged improper return home of a patient from the Brook Fever Hospital in an infected state on 6th January, and his return being followed by the removal of the three other children with scarlet fever1 on the 11th and 13th January, and complaints of similar cases having been made to the Metropolitan Asylums Board by the Stepney Borough Council, the Public Health and Housing Committee were of opinion serious attention should be directed to the matter, the discharge of patients in a 74 condition to convey infection to other persons constituting a grave danger to public health, a source of unnecessary suffering, and of additional expense to patients, their friends, and the public. The Committee recommended the Council to endorse their opinion, and the Metropolitan Asylums Board be requested to take such steps as may be necessary to prevent in future the discharge of patients while in a condition liable to convey infection. In accordance with the directions of the Council, the Town Clerk addressed a letter to other Metropolitan Borough Councils giving the facts of the above cases, and stating the reply of the Metropolitan Asylums Board: "That they are unable to obtain any definite evidence clearly associating the admission of the other members of the family with his discharge from Brook Hospital." The Town Clerk, in his circular letter to the Metropolitan Boroughs, inquired "whether any cases of infection, or suspected infection, from the premature discharge of patients from hospitals of the Metropolitan Asylums Boards have occurred in your Borough, and to ask you to be good enough to bring the matter to the notice of your Council, with a view to pressure being brought to bear upon the Metropolitan Asylums Board to secure the adoption of more adequate precautions against premature discharges and consequent spread of infection." Various replies were received, some Boroughs had had similar experience to the Poplar Borough, and some had not. The Committee recommended the Council: "That the attention of the Local Government Board be directed to the matter, and they be urged to take the necessary steps to enforce adequate precautions being in future taken to prevent the discharge of patients from Metropolitan Asylums Board Hospitals while in a condition likely to convey infection to other persons." The following circular letter was; received from the Metropolitan Asylums Board: — 75 "Metropolitan Asylums Board, Embankment, E.C., 17th June, 190Jf. Sir, RETURN CASES OF SCARLET FEVER. The attention of the Managers has been called to the fact that a circular letter has been issued by the Poplar Borough Council with reference to an alleged premature discharge of a patient from one of the Board's Hospitals. Without entering upon the merits of the particular case referred to, I have been requested to bring the following facts under the notice of your Council. At the present time there is an entire want of precise knowledge both as to the causation of scarlet fever and also as to the period of infectivity of patients suffering from the disease, and in the absence of definite scientific knowledge it is impossible for anyone to assert positively that infection has or has not ceased in any given case. Until recently there was a consensus of opinion that the desquamation which occurs in such cases was coincident with the infectivity of the patient, and that the cessation of desquamation could, therefore, be taken as an indication to determine this latter point; but the Board understands that this opinion has undergone considerable modification during recent years, it being held by many of the more prominent physicians, medical officers of health, superintendents of fever hospitals, and other medical authorities that desquamation, especially that in the latter stages, cannot be taken as evidence of infectivity. The Board have long been aware that so-called return cases have from time to time occurred, and with the object of obtaining the fullest information as to their cause, and with the view of adopting (if practicable) further precautions, they three years since appointed a medical officer to conduct investigations into the circumstances of all such alleged cases, and the report of this officer will shortly be in print. As the result of this and other steps which the Board are taking they trust that they may be enabled to throw much light upon this at present obscure subject. 76 Notwithstanding this absence of scientific knowledge, the Board are confident that the most careful judgment is exercised in every case by the medical officers whom they have placed in charge of their hospitals. The Board are of course alive to the responsible duties which rest upon them, and they can assure the Council that they are taking every means rightly to discharge these, both as regards the patients and the public. I am, Sir, Your obedient Servant, T. DUNCOMBE MANN, Clerk to the Board. To the Town Clerk of the Borough of Poplar. CONTINUED AND TYPHUS FEVERS. Thirty-four specimens of blood from doubtful cases of Typhoid Fever were forwarded to the Lister Institute of Preventive Medicine. In 6 instances (17.6 per cent.) the Typhoid (Widal) reaction was obtained. One Poplar parishioner died from Typhoid Fever, 7 fatal 80 cases occurred among the Bromley patients, and 5 among the Bow, making a total of 13 deaths in 80 cases. As is usual it will be seen that the greater number of Typhoid Fever cases occurred during the latter half of the year. 1st Quarter. 2nd Quarter. 3rd Quarter. 4th Quarter. Total. Measles and Homework. Sie also page 38. The following figures are the deaths from measles for the past twelve years: — Poplar. Bromley. Bow. Borough of Poplar. 1893 12 20 8 40 1894 58 95 57 210 1895 82 105 25 212 1896 49 32 62 143 1897 33 35 12 80 1898 34 53 38 125 1899 39 44 17 100 1900 25 28 20 73 1901 26 38 25 89 1902 31 27 10 68 1903 53 88 20 161 1904 30 46 14 90 For death rates see Tables XIV. and XV., page 25. An "Order" was made by the London County Council applying to Measles in the Administrative County of London, the provisions 85 of sections 60, 61, 62, 63, 64, 65, 68, 69, 70, 72; 73 and 74; of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, with respect to dangerous infectious diseases. The "Order" was duly approved by the Local Government Board, and came into force from and including 1st April, 1903. Practically the Infectious Diseases Prevention Sections, and not the Notification Sections, of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, apply to Measles; or in other words, the effect of the "Order" is that the precautions taken in the case of notifiable diseases with regard to disinfection and the exposure of infected persons are now applied to Measles, but the provisions of the Act with regard to notification and removal do not apply. Arrangements are made upon the receipt of a notice from the Head Teachers of a School, or any other person, of the existence of Measles in a house, for the Sanitary Inspector to call, and leave the same papers as would be left when a notification is received at the Public Health Offices. Upon the Measles Order coming into force on the 1st April, 1903, a Measles Register was instituted, in which entries are made of premises where cases are stated to exist. The bulk of the cases thus dealt with has been upon information received at the hands of School Teachers; by notification, as provided for, by the School Management Code of the London County Council, and the remainder from various sources, i.e., through medical practitioners, parents, landlords, lodgers, neighbours, police, and in fatal cases by the Registrar's Returns, these latter in some instances being the first intimation received of the existence of the disease upon the premises. The details in the Register cover a variety of information, viz., date, name, address, source of notification, number of rooms disinfected, details as to articles disinfected, etc., and remarks. 86 At the end of the year there were 868 entries in the Register, representing the number of premises where Measles had been stated to have occurred. Upon enquiry 10 cases were stated to have recovered, 158 were found not to be cases, but 54 of these cases were stated to be suffering from a different disease other than the one notified, 43 wrong addresses and unknown were given, 3 belonged to other Boroughs, and 33 moved away before enquiries could be made. So that practically as far as is known, 627 cases of Measles occurred in the Borough. Eight cases of Measles occurred in connection with milk shops. Three hundred rooms were disinfected by the Public Health Department, and the remainder were disinfected by the tenants, medical practitioners giving certificates that disinfection had been satisfactorily carried out. Respecting the articles disinfected by the Council, see page 54. Owing to the prevalence of Measles in the Infants' Department of the Fairfield Road Board School, the Medical Officer of the School Board advised the closure of Class-room B. for a period of two weeks. The class-room was disinfected by the Public Health Department. The Head Teacher of the Infants' Department, Cubitt Town Board School, wrote on the 9th March that "In accordance with a telegram received from the Clerk of the London School Board this afternoon, I have closed the baby-room till after Easter. A serious outbreak of measles has occurred in the baby-class this week." The Babies' Room was closed for a period of three weeks from the 9th March, and was disinfected by the Public Health Department. Owing to the prevalence of Measles in the Infants' Department of the Oban Street School, the School Medical Officer of the 87 London County Council advised the closure of Class-room A for a period of three weeks from the 25th April. The Medical Officer of the Educational Department of the London County Council was communicated with on 17th May, respecting the prevalence of measles at the Woolmore Street School, but it was not considered closure of the school would be effective as the "second crop" had gone. Owing to the prevalence of Measles in the Infants' Department of the Tidey Street School, certain class-rooms were closed from the 17th May until after the Whitsuntide holidays, and disinfection of the class and cloak-rooms was carried out by the Public Health Department. Owing to the prevalence of Measles in the Infants' Department of the Tidey Street School, a letter was received on the 3rd November from the Medical Officer (Education) London County Council, stating he had advised the closure of Class-room B. until the 14th November. MEASLES.—HOMEWORK: (See page 38). On the 6th of May I reported to the Public Health and Housing Committee: — With respect to section 110 of the Factory and Workshop Act, 1901, "prohibition of homework in places where there is an infectious disease," this section does not apply in the case of measles, for by sub-section 5 of section 110 the Infectious Diseases to which this section applies are the Infectious Diseases required to be notified under the law for the time being in force in relation to the notification of Infectious Diseases, and the Infectious Diseases in London are expressed in sub-section 8, Section 55, of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891. Factory and Workshop Act, 1901. 110 (5). The infectious diseases to which this section applies are the infectious diseases required to be notified under the law 88 for the time being in force in relation to the notification of infectious diseases, and the work to which this section applies is the making, cleaning, washing, altering, ornamenting, finishing and repairing of wearing apparel and any work incidental thereto and such other classes of work as may be specified by special order of the Secretary of State. Public Health (London) Act, 1891. Infectious Diseases.— Prevention. 58. The following provisions of this Act relating to dangerous infectious diseases shall apply to the infectious diseases specifically mentioned in, the foregoing enactment of this Act relating to the notification of infectious disease, and all or any of such provisions may be applied by order to any other infectious disease in the same manner as that enactment may be applied to such disease, subject to the same power of revoking and varying the order, and every such infectious disease is in this Act referred to as a dangerous infectious disease. The sections of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, which are made to apply to Measles under section 58 of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, by the order of the London County Council, approved by the Local Government Board are 60, 61, 62, 63, 64, 65, 68, 69, 70, 72, 73, 74, so that clothing can be made in a house where Measles exists, and can only be dealt with under section 68 of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891. It would appear that sub-section 5, sec. 110, of the Factory and Workshops Act, 1901, requires amending by inserting after the words " for the time being in force in relation to the notification of infectious diseases," the following words: "and any other diseases included under section 58 of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, by an order of the London County Council or Sanitary Authority, approved by the Local Government. Board." It was suggested to the Committee that the attention of the Home Office, the Local Government Board and the London County Council be called to this section. 89 The Committee were of opinion section 110 (5) of the Factory and Workshop Act, 1901, should be amended, so that home work on wearing apparel may be prohibited where measles or other infectious disease, to which any of the infectious diseases clauses of the Public Health (London) Act have been applied by order of the London County Council, exists in the same manner as in the case of the infectious diseases specified in section 55 (8) of the Public Health (London) Act. The Committee recommended the Council "That the Council concur in this opinion, and the representations on the subject be made to the Home Secretary, the President of the Local Government Board and the London County Council." Four mortuaries are possessed by the Council: — Bickmore Street, Poplar Originally provided by the Overseers of the respective parishes for the reception of bodies cast ashore or found in the streets. St. Leonards Road, Bromley Bow Church, Bow Wellington Road Originally provided by the Poplar District Board for the reception of bodies of persons who died from infectious diseases. Pending the provision of a proper mortuary, that at Bickmore Street has been used as a mortuary and post-mortem room under the Public Health (London) Act. The Council is required by the Public Health (London) Act 1891, to provide a mortuary and may make bye-laws with respect to the management and charges for the use of same. If required by the London County Council separate accommodation for the reception of dead bodies, during the time required to make postmortem examinations ordered by the Coroner, shall be provided. 92 The London County Council is required to provide proper accommodation for the holding of inquests, and may by agreement with the Borough Council provide in connection with a mortuary a building for post-mortem examinations provided by that authority, on terms to be agreed upon. The Public Health and Housing Committee recommended the Council 'to acquire the land in Glaucus Street adjoining the stone yard for the erection of a shelter and disinfecting station as well as a coroner's court and mortuary. The Council adopted the recommendation of the Committee. Later on, the Public Control Committee of the London County Council agreed to recommend the Council to approve the provision by the Poplar Borough Council of a Coroner's Court in connection with mortuary, etc., proposed to be erected in Glaucus Street. The special form submitted by the Secretary of State for the guidance of Medical Officers of Health {see page 101) in preparing the report on factories and workshops in pursuance of section 132 of the Factory and Workshop Act 1901, did not come to hand until the end of the year, and as far as possible, from the records, journals, registers and index files kept, the return has been prepared. In order that the form may be filled in without any difficulty from year to year, the Sanitary Inspectors hare been urgently requested to give full and complete descriptions of the premises visited when any work is carried on, and to classify such premises in their note books before handing them to the clerks for the particulars to be entered in the Journals, registers and index files.* It can be easily understood that the classification of premises where work is executed will vary from time to time, according to the pressure of work or the nature of the work carried oil—outworkers premises may become domestic workshops, and also workshops, and even factories; workshops may become factories; and workplaces may become workshops or factories, between the visits of the Sanitary Inspectors. Whilst this report is in course of preparation, many workshops and workplaces not hitherto registered are being added to the respective registers and will consequently appear in the Annual Report for 1905.
