This sample was taken on demand by the Inspector. The question for legal proceedings was whether the package was properly marked. The Committee directed a letter of caution be sent to the vendor. In the case of the prosecution of the vendor of the sample of milk (see legal proceedings**, page 110) deficient in fat and solids not fat to the extent of 15 per cent, and 5 per cent. respectively, notice was received on behalf of the vendor of intention to rely on the warranty given by the farmer supplying the milk. The contract containing the warranty in question was dated September, 1903, and it appeared from cases already decided, a specific warranty with each delivery is not necessary to afford protection against conviction of the vendor, and no proceedings could have been taken against the farmer, as such proceedings must have been taken within six months of the datei of the warranty, so that under these circumstances further action was abandoned. In any case it is almost impossible to obtain convictions where warranties are set up in defence, since no proof can be given that the milk is not sold in the same state as received by tha vendor, and the persons giving the warranty would affirmi they had reason to believe the milk supplied was in accordance with the. warranty given. The attention of the Committee was called to similar cases which had occurred in other London Boroughs, and to the immunity from conviction under the sale of Food and Drugs Acts, which is being increasingly secured by vendors and dealers, by means of such warranties. H 114 The Paddington Borough Council and the Woolwich Borough Council appealed to the Local Government Board and the Board of Agriculture to promote legislation in the matter, the Woolwich Council suggesting the repeal of the Warranty Sections of the Sale of Food and Drugs Acts, leaving the person selling the adulterated article to his common law remedy against the person from whom he purchased for damages for breach of warranty. The Public Health and Housing Committee were of opinion legislation on the matter is urgently required, and that so long as vendors are enabled to rely upon a warranty as a defence against charges of adulteration, the conviction of offenders will become increasingly difficult to obtain. The Committee recommended to the Council that the Local Government Board and the Board of Agriculture be urged to promote legislation for the repeal of the clauses of the Sale of Food and Drugs Acts enabling warranties to be set up in defence in cases of prosecution of vendors of adulterated articles; or for an amendment of the law so as to require the retail dealer relying upon a warranty to bring before the Court for conviction in his stead the person giving the warranty. The Camberwell Borough Council addressed a communication to the Local Government Board requesting them to take such steps as may be necessary in order that a limit may be fixed to the amount of boracic acid which may be mixed with food. At present there is no standard with regard to the amount of preservative which should be allowed. The Committee recommended the Council to support the application of the Camberwell Borough Council to the Local Government Board. A letter was received from the Board of Agriculture forwarding the following extract from the report of one of their Inspectors on the subject of the number of samples taken under the Sale of Food and Drugs Acts, in the Borough, for analysis, and requesting the observations of the Council thereon:— 115 [Extract.] Date, 3rd February, 1904. Sale of Food and Drugs Acts, 1875 to 1S99. Local Authority, POPLAR. 1900. 1901. 1902. 1903. Exmd. Adult. Exmd. Adult. Exmd. Adult. Exmd. Adult. Total 342 26 362 33 369 57 373 31 Milk 122 13 106 12 151 34 140 Butter 50 8 83 9 86 14 109 The above statement shows the total number of samples and the number of milk and butter samples taken in the Borough of Poplar in each year since 1900. Having regard to the large number of articles liable to adulteration, or which are likely to be sold under a false description, the Board of Agriculture considered the number of samples hitherto taken could scarcely be regarded as sufficient to test adequately the food supply of the district. The letter stated: Many local Authorities consider it necessary to take annually a number which will give a proportion of at least 3 per 1,000 of the population, while the Local Authorities of some of the Metropolitan Boroughs cause a much larger number than this to be taken. For instance, during the year 1902, 1,250 samples were taken in the City of London (population 26,923); 1,802 in Westminster (population 117,178); 1,935 in Southwark (population 206,180); 402 in Holborn (population 59,405); and 759 in Bermondsey (population 130,760); and the general tendency is in the direction of a still further increase. The communication drew the attention of the Poplar Borough Council to the obligations imposed upon them by sub-section 1 h 2 116 of Section 3 of the Act of 1899, and the responsibilities vested in the Central Authority by sub-section 2 of the same section; and the Board of Agriculture asked the Poplar Borough Council to consider whether it would not be desirable to give directions for a larger number of samples per annum to be taken for analysis. The Sections of the Act referred to above are as follows:— 3.—(1.) It shall be the duty of every local authority entrusted with the execution of the laws relating to the sale of food and drugs to appoint a public analyst, and put in force from time to time, as occasion may arise, the powers with which they are invested, so as to provide proper securities for the sale of food and drugs in a pure and genuine condition, and in particular to direct their officers to take samples for analysis. (2.) If the Local Government Board or Board of Agriculture, after communication with a Local Authority, are of opinion that the Local Authority have failed to execute or enforce any of the provisions of the Sale of Food and Drugs Acts in relation to any article of food, and that their failure affects the general interest of the consumer or the general interests of agriculture in the United Kingdom, as the case may be, the Board concerned may, by order, empower an officer of the Board to execute and enforce those provisions or to procure the execution and enforcement thereof in relation to any article of food mentioned in the order. Sub-section 3 provides that the expenses incurred under any order made under sub-section 2 shall be paid by the local authority. The Board of Agriculture considered that at least three samples per annum should be taken per 1,000 of the population. The present number in Poplar is 2.2 per 1,000, which, with the exception of St. Pancras (2.1) is the lowest in London, the average —exclusive of the City—being 3.7. At the present time the Council is in communication with the Local Government Board respecting the work of analysing samples under the Food and Drugs and Margarine Acts. 117 unsound food and examination of food stuffs at wharves, etc. The attention of the Public Health and Housing Committee was called to the "Sanitary Record" of the 27th October, in which the following article appeared:— "Unsound Meat. "We understand that the meat trades are seeking an amendment of the law, to shift from the owner or trader the onus of proving that meat seized as being unsound was not. intended for human food, and to place the burden of proof that it was so intended upon the Local Authorities or prosecuting Inspector. We have not learned what steps have been taken v/ith this object, but we have no hesitation in saying that it would be a most unwise and retrograde change in the law. It would be detrimental to the public health and public policy, and we cannot believe that there is any probability of its being accepted by Parliament. The Corporation of Glasgow are moving in the matter, with the intention of opposing the suggested amendment of the law." It was reported to the Committee that in the case, which was decided at the Thames Police Court on the 28th of October, Langley v. Carle, when the defendant was fined £10 10s. ard £5 5s. costs, Mr. Dickinson, the magistrate, stated, when innict ing the penalty, he hoped that this case would be a lesson ta the trade to examine meat, either upon purchase at the market or immediately upon it being delivered at the premises of a butcher, as it was easy enough for a butcher to state, as in the case before him when an article had been seized, thatf he (thel butcher) would have found the article to be bad when he was about to use it. The offence in the case of Langley v. Carle was aggravated by the fact that the heart and liver which were attached to the diseased lungs had been cut off for sale, and the lungs hung up in the sausage and faggot factory, and not put on one side. The lungs in question belonged to a pig, and were no doubt tuberculous and one of the recommendations of the Royal Commission on 118 Tuberculosis was that when any part of a pig is tuberculous the whole carcase should be destroyed. At present the law stands as follows: Public Health (London) Act, 1891. SECTION 47. (1) the proof that the same was not exposed or deposited for any such purpose or was not intended for the food of man, resting with the person charged; (3) Where it is shown that any article liable to be seized under this section, and found in the possession of any person was purchased by him from another person for the food of man, and when so purchased was in such a condition as 10 be liable to be seized and condemned under this section, the person who so sold the same shall be liable to the fine and imprisonment above mentioned, unless he proves that at the time he sold the said article he did not know, and had no reason to believe, that it was in such condition. (8) Where a person has in his possession any article which is unsound op unwholesome or unfit for the food of man, he may, by written notice to the sanitary authority, specifying such article, and containing a sufficient identification of it, request its removal, and the sanitary authority shall cause it to be removed as if it were trade refuse. This last subjection clearly shows that; a butcher should examine all his meat immediately upon arrival at his premises, and put on one side any which is diseased or unsound, or unwholesome, or unfit for the food of man, and should not run the risk of an officer of a sanitary authority doing it for him, and further, taking such steps as the law empowers the officer to do. It was suggested to the Committee that they recommend the Council to ask the London County Council to oppose any such alteration in the law as the question is one which affects the whole of London, and in fact, the country generally. It was also suggested that the attention of the Borough's Members of Parliament might also be called to the matter. 119 The Committee directed that the Medical Officer's report on the subject of unsound food be sent to the London County Council and to the local Members of Parliament. With respect to the examination of food stuffs see Sanitary Inspectors reports, pages 173, 181, 192, 197, 203, 218 and 223. Also, with respect to the examination of food stuffs at wharves, see Inspector's reports, pages 203, 218 and 223. The Committee considered reports showing that an increasing quantity of fish purchased by small dealers at Billingsgate is found an hour or two afterwards to be in a condition unfit for food, and is brought to the Council's Inspectors to be condemned. The Committee were of opinion more supervision is needed at Billingsgate to prevent decomposing fish being sent out of the market for human consumption, and directed that communications on the subject be addressed to the Markets Committee of the City Corporation and to the Fishmongers' Company. See Mr. Field's report, page 182. clean milk. A report was received from the Medical Officer of Health for Islington on "Clean Milk," and a copy of a letter referring to the report, which was sent to the London County Council by the Borough of Islington, in which communication it is stated: "It is understood that the London County Council propose to apply to Parliament for powers enabling them as the authority dealing with the County of London to secure at their own expense analyses of samples of milk arriving at metropolitan railway stations for distribution in the county, the letter suggests that the London County Council should at the same time obtain powers to prevent the introduction into the Metropolis of milk containing sediment of a dirty character, such as that referred to in the report. The Borough of Islington is communicating with the Local Government Board with a view to the better enforcement outside London of the Dairies, Cowsheds and Milkshops Order, and of the regulations made thereunder, but the Islington Borough Council are of opinion 120 that the London County Council might with advantage apply to Parliament for such additional powers as will enable them to inspect dairies, cowsheds and cattle from which milk is supplied to the County of London, or as an alternative for powers to prevent the introduction of milk from such districts as do not enforce the aforesaid Order and regulations. I reported to the Committee the case would be met if the Dairies, Cowsheds and Milkshops Order and the regulations made thereunder be enforced strictly by local authorities outside the Metropolis, and instead of it being optional for local authorities to make regulations, as per article 13 of the Dairies, Cowsheds and Milkshops Order, it should be compulsory, and the Local Government Board and the various County Councils should see that the regulations are enforced. Article 13 mentioned above is as follows:— A Local Authority MAY from time to time make Regulations for the following purposes, or any of them: (a) For the inspection of cattle in dairies. (b) for prescribing and regulating the lighting, ventilation, cleansing, drainage and water supply of dairies and cowsheds in the occupation of persons following the trade of cowkeepers or dairymen. (c) For securing the cleanliness of milk-stores, milk-shops and of milk-vessels used for containing milk for sale by such persons. (d) For prescribing precautions to be taken by purveyors of milk and persons selling milk by retail against infection or contamination. In a circular letter of the Local Government Board to Councils of Boroughs and Urban Districts, dated 11th March, 1889, it is stated:— "If the Council have not already made regulations under the Order of 1885, the Board think that they should do so, and 121 that any such regulations would, with advantage, be based on the Model Clause. If the Council have already made regulations under the Order, the Model Clauses may be usefully considered in connection with any fresh regulations or amendment of the existing code which the Council may propose to make." The Public Health and Housing Committee considered the proposal of the Islington Borough Couilci! that the London County Council should be urged to obtain powers to inspect dairies, cowsheds and cattle outside London and from which London is supplied with milk, with a view to ensuring the cleanliness of such milk. The Committee also considered my report, and concurred in the opinion such powers are inadvisable, but that the making of bye-laws under the Cowsheds and Milkshops Order, 1885, should be made/ compulsory, instead of being optional as at present, and that the several Authorities should be required to enforce regulations made under that Order. The Committee recommended the Council to endorse such opinion, and the Local Government Board, the London County Council and the Islington Borough Council be so informed. bread acts. 3 George IV., Cap. CVI., 6 and 7, Will. 4, C. 37. The following letter was received from the London County Council onl the subject of the law relating to the sale of bread:— "LONDON COUNTY COUNCIL. " County hall, Spring Gardens, S.W. 10th February, 1904. ''Sir " A Committee of the Council has under consideration the question of the desirableness of the Council seeking fresh legislation for the better protection of the public than is afforded under the existing law as regards the sale of bread; and it has been suggested 122 that the best course to be adopted would be to endeavour to obtain the repeal of the Act of 3 George IV., Chapter cvi., so far as its operation in the Administrative County of London is concerned, and to get re-enacted in modern language such of the provisions of that Act as are still valuable in present circumstances; and also to obtain additional provisions to afford the desired increase of protection to the public. "I am to state that the Committee will be glad if the Metropolitan Borough Council will be good enough to assist in the matter by stating whether, in its opinion, any of the provisions in the above-mentioned Act are of value in connection with the prevention of adulteration, etc., and should, therefore, be re-enacted, or whether in view of the powers bestowed by the Sale of Food and Drugs Act, 1875, the provisions of the former Act as regards adulteration have become obsolete and might, with advantage, be repealed. "I am, Sir, ''Your obedient Servant, "G. L. GOMME, "Clerk of the Council. "The Town Clerk, " Metropolitan Borough of Poplar." I reported on the matter as follows: — Bread is made and sold in the City of London and Liberties thereof and within 10 miles of the Royal Exchange under the Act of 3 Geo. IV., Chap. CVI., which was passed on 22nd July, 1822, and outside the 10 mile radius under the Bread Act of 6 and 7 Will. 4, c. 37, which was passed on the 28th July, 1836. The statutes are almost identical. If the Bread Act of Geo. IV. be repealed and the adulteration sections re-enacted, the Medical Officer would suggest that the adulteration provisions of the Act should be enforced by the Sanitary Authority. The adulteration sections of the Geo. IV. Act 123 being sections II., X., XI., XII., and XIII., and the penalty provisions which follow should be re-enacted in modern language. A section should be inserted to the effect that meal or flour of wheat, barley, rye, oats, buck-wheat, Indian corn, peas, beans, rice or potatoes Should be separately stored in properly marked sacks or bins. The properly appointed officers of the local authority should have the right to enter any premises and without purchasing take any samples of dough or bread, or samples of meal or flour of any grain from any sacks or bins and submit the same to the public analyst, and if found to be adulterated, proceedings should be taken. The taking of samples in this manner, without purchasing, would not be a new procedure, as Inspectors have power, for the purposes of analysis, to take without purchasing samples purporting to be butter and cheese. Butter, under section 10, Margarine Act, 1887, 50 and 51 Vict., c. 29, and cheese under Food and Drugs Amendment Act, 62 and 63 Vict., c. 51, section 5. The baker should be protected by warranty in a like manner as the vendor is protected under the Food and Drugs, and Margarine Acts, and then sections X., XI., XII., of Act 3 Geo. IV., CVI., would if re-enacted be well met. The warranty would also apply to section XII., so as to protect those persons who sell, but do not make bread. So far as section XII. is also concerned, which enacts that bread made of mixed meal or flour is to be marked with a large Roman M, it would be better that when bread is made wholly or partially of the meal or flour of any other sort of corn or grain than wheat, or of the meal or flour of any peas or beans, to have all such bread marked or stamped with the name of its component parts, so as to be intelligible to the public who do not know what a large Roman M means. Potatoes should be added to this section, as these ingredients are included in 6, 7, Will. 4, c. 37, sec. 10, which is similar to 3 124 Geo. IV., chap. CVI., sec. 12, but there is a proviso in section 10 of the Bread Act of Will. IV. which can be added, and is as follows:—"Provided always, that nothing in this Act contained shall extend or be construed to extend to require any bread made of the meal or flour of wheat only, and in the making of which potato yeast shall be used, to be marked as hereinbefore mentioned." The powers of entry should be on the same lines as those proposed in the Bill of the London County Council (General Powers), clause 27:—"The sanitary authority of any sanitary district, or any officer or person duly authorised by them in that behalf, shall have the right to enter at all reasonable times any house, building or premises in that district for the purpose of examining whether there is any contravention of this part of this Act or of any Byelaw made thereunder, or for the purpose of carrying into execution this part of this Act or any Bye-law made thereunder." The Public Health and Housing Committee examined the Bread Act of 1822 and considered the report thereon, and recommended the Council that the suggestions be approved and submitted to the London County Council. SALE OF HORSE FLESH, ETC., REGULATION ACT, 1889, 52 & 53 VICT CH. II. A report was presented to the Committee respecting the necessity for an alteration in the law relating to the sale of horse flesh. Among other matters it was pointed out to the Committee that if horse flesh specially prepared be passed through a mincing machine and mixed with fat, flesh and spice, it cannot be distinguished from that of ox, nor can the nature of the flesh, in which under normal circumstances the products of disease would be readily detected, be always distinguished even by the expert. By section 6 of the 1889 Act, a penalty of a fine only can be inflicted, the maximum being twenty pounds. 125 The report suggested the law should be so altered that a term of imprisonment (six months with or without hard labour) or a fine not exceeding fifty pounds, may be imposed at the discretion of the magistrate. The fine or imprisonment should be applicable in respect of each separate portion of horse flesh as it is in the case of every article of unsound food under section 47 (2) of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891. The other alterations which suggested themselves were, that every knacker and every wholesale dealer in horse flesh shall be registered by the Local Authority, and shall keep a register showing the quantity and destination of each consignment sent out from his premises, and this register shall be open to the inspection of any duly appointed officer of the Local Authority, and no meat shall be sent out from any horse slaughterers unless thoroughly cooked through, and all parcels sent out shall be labelled with the words " horse flesh." The Public Health and Housing Committee recommended the Council to address communications to the President of the Local Government Board, and the London County Council urging legislation on the subject. HOUSING OF THE WORKING CLASSES. In July I represented as "Unhealthy Areas," to the London County Council under part 1 of the Housing of the Working Classes Act, 1890, two areas South of the Bow Road known as (1) "Brewery Yard Area" comprising 21 dwelling-houses,—as rated. (2) " Gandy Court Area," comprising 14 dwelling-houses. The London County Coulncil decided not to entertain my representations. Summonses were applied for at the Thames Police Court in respect of Nos. 4, 5, 6, 7, 13 and 14, Brewery Yard, Bow, under Section 8 (1) of the Housing of the Working Classes Act, 1903. Under this section the summonses were taken out without notices 126 being served, as it was considered the premises were in such a state that the occupation thereof should be immediately discontinued. It was also very questionable whether the premises were reasonably capable of being made fit for human habitation. The summonses were heard on the 16th December, but on account of the conflicting evidence by the defendant's architect and builder on the one hand, and the Medical Officer of Health on the other, the Magistrate adjourned his decision until the 30th December, in order that he might inspect the houses. The Magistrate had been asked to view both by the defendant's solicitor and myself. The Magistrate (Mr. Dickinson) in giving his decision, said: " Their state is disgraceful, and quite unfit for human habitation." Closing orders were made, and 25s. costs allowed in each case. Allowance was asked for the tenants on account of expenses in removing. The Magistrate authorised 10s. to be paid to each tenant of Nos. 4, 5, 6 and 13, and 15s. to the tenant of No. 14. A total of £10 5s., costs and allowances. Notices were served upon the tenants to cease occupying the houses by the 12th January. After the decision of the Magistrate in the above instances, I presented a report to the Committee as to how the law stands in respect of the demolition of dwelling-houses after closing orders had been made, and certain amendments required to the Acts were also pointed out, and *I suggested that the attention of the London County Council might be called to my remarks, as they might induce the London County Council to reconsider "representationn " which had been made to them. The Committee directed communications to be addressed to the London County Council and the Local Government Board with reference to the matter. At the date of finishing this report, 11th March, 1905, the Housing of the Working Classes Committee of the London County Council are about to lay before the London County Council certain proposals in respect of my " representations." 127 Hurfords's Court, Tucker's Court and Favonia Street Area. These areas are now being cleared by the London County Council. "The scheme, which was confirmed by Act of Parliament passed in July, 1900, comprises three small areas in the Metropolitan Borough of Poplar. The Burford's Court and Tucker's Court areas will be sold for commercial purposes, and the Favonia Street area will almost entirely be laid out as a playground. By the clearance of the areas 269 persons will be displaced, and accommodation for them is being provided on a site about half a mile distant in Preston's Road, the greater part of which is being utilised by arrangement with the School Board for London for fulfilling the re-housing obligations of the Board. The gross cost of the scheme is estimated at £16,000, and the net cost, inclusive of that of laying out the playground at £13,300. All interests in the property have been acquired by the Council, 14 out of 19 claims received having been settlod by agreement. During the past year the arbitrator appointed by the Secretary of State to deal with thef disputed claims issued his award, the amount claimed being £7,457 8s. in all, and the total awarded £3,455." Providence Place Area. Certain portions of this area (Union Place and Court) have been boarded up by the London County Council. "The Providence Place scheme provides for the clearance of an area nearly one acre in extent, lying south of the East India Dock Road, and east of Cotton Street, Poplar, and incidentally for the extension of Union Street to Woolmore Street, and the widening of a length of that road on its western side. The houses are for the most part two-storey cottages, situated in Providence Place and adjacent courts, Providence Place itself being a narrow court about 10 feet wide leading out of Woolmore Street. During the past year the scheme has been slightly modified by the exclusion of the site of the out buildings on the southern side of Union Court at the rear of Nos. 24, 25 and 26, Ashton Street. The scheme was 128 undertaken by the Council on 29th October, 1901, and 361 persons of the working class will be displaced, while dwellings to accomodate about 400 persons can be erected on the cleared area. The gross cost of clearance is estimated at £17,000, the cost of works at £1,600, and the value of the cleared area for housing purposes at £1,500, thus leaving the net cost of the scheme at £17,100. All interests in the property have already been acquired by the Council, and the question of re-development of the area is now receiving attention." Bridge Road and Emmett Street. "This area, which was the subject of an official representation, dated 12th July, 1901, by Dr. F. W. Alexander, the Medical Officer of Health of the Metropolitan Borough of Poplar, is situated between Bridge Road and Emmett Street, and comprises Providence Cottages, four houses in Emmett Street, and two blocks of four and five-storey buildings known as Arnolds Buildings. These buildings which contained aboutl 110 tenements and between 300 and 400 rooms, were erected in 1888, but were closed in 1894, by a magistrate's order. The area did not appear to the Council to be one which should be1 dealt with under Part I. of the Housing of the Working Classes Act, 1890, but we were of opinion that Arnold's Buildings should be demolished. In view, however, of a proposal by the owners to convert the premises into a workmen's hotel, no action was taken in that direction for a time, but as the owners had failed to effect any alterations a magistrate's order was obtained on 25th March, 1904, for the demolition of the buildings within a period of six weeks from that date." The houses in Eagle Place and Prospect Place still remain closed and the entrances to these cul de sacs are boarded up. 129 HOUSES LET IN LODGINGS. The Committee had under consideration judgment of the High Court (King's Bench Division) on appeal from decisions of Police Magistrates in connection with the enforcement of the By-laws of the Boroughs of Stepney and Islington relating to Houses Let in Lodgings. The charge in both cases was that the landlord of a lodging-house did not in the first week in April cause every part of the premises to be cleansed as required by the by-laws, and in one case the summons was dismissed, and in the other the defendant was fined. The Lord Chief Justice, in delivering judgment in both cases, stated that in his opinion the by-laws were bad, because they did not provide that the persons who were made responsible for" seeing that the things were done should receive the necessary notice. In his opinion the by-laws required remodelling, with the point of view not of removing the liability of the landlord but of giving him notice before subjecting him to penalties. The By-laws of the Poplar Borough Council were similar to the by-laws in question in that there was no provision for notice to be given to landlords to cleanse the premises before proceedings were taken for penalties, although in practice such notice was invariably given. A communication was addressed by thei Commitee to the Local Government Board with a view to settling a form of amendment of the existing by-laws to provide for notice to be given. The Council approved the action of the Committee. The Local Government Board submitted amended model bylaws and suggested the Council should consider the desirability of amending their by-laws in a corresponding manner, which was accordingly done by the Council on the 2nd June, and the Local Government Board confirmed the by-laws on tiie 7th September. The clauses lelating to the service of notices and penalties in the new by-laws are as follows: — I 130 "19. Where a duty imposed upon or a prohibition applied to the landlord of a lodging-house is by any by-law expressly imposed or applied subject to the provisions of these by-laws, proceedings shall not be taken against the landlord for an offence against the by-law unless and until the landlord, after service upon him of a notice in writing by the Council requiring him within such reasonable time as is specified in the notice to comply with the by-law, has failed to comply with the by-law within the time so specified. Penalties. "20. Every person who shall offend against any of the foregoing by-laws shall be liable for every such offence to a penalty of five pounds, and in the case of a continuing offence to a further penalty of forty shillings for each day after written notice of the offence from the Council: "Provided, nevertheless, that the justices or court before whom any complaint may be made or any proceedings may be taken in respect of any such offence may, if they think fit, adjudge the payment, as a penalty, of any sum less than the full amount of the penalty imposed by this by-law." At the end of the year 1904, there were 768 houses on the register. See the report of the Inspector (Mr. Wm. Johnson) for Houses Let in Lodgings, page 228, as to the work executed under the by-laws. SEWERAGE AND DRAINAGE. In the memorandum issued by the Local Government Board to the Medical Officers of Health respecting their Annual Reports, it is stated that the Local Government Board desire to obtain, through the Annual Reports of the Medical Officers of Health, information of particular changes of condition that occur by the action of the Local Authority, and one of the subject matters which is required to be reported upon is the condition of sewer and house drains; therefore, one cannot do better than report upon the excellent work which has been carried out by the Public Health and Housing Committee at the instigation of the Borough Surveyor. 131 Mr. Heckford, the Borough Surveyor, reported that it was desirable a new sewer be constructed in Carpenters Road to take the sewage from that portion of the Borough between Hackney Cut Navigation and the River Lea. Messrs. Clarke, Nickolls and Coombs had carried out building operations at the place in question and had called upon the Council to arrange for the periodical removal of soil from the cesspit connection with their works. This the Council is required to do under the Public Health (London) Act, and in view of this and the probable erection of other premises requiring similar facilities, it was considered desirable the sewer should be constructed. The Surveyor proposed to construct a 12-inch pipe sewer, with syphon in duplicate, under the Hackney Cut Navigation, and to connect the system with the existing sewer in Hepscott Road. The Woodstock Road sewer, passing under 146, High Street, Poplar, and discharging into the Black Ditch sewer at the rear, having become blocked, the surveyor proposed that the sewer from Woodstock Road should be cut off and taken into the London County Council High School sewer, and that the portion of sewer beneath the house should be demolished and re-laid in 6-inch pipes with fall to High Street. The Borough Surveyor reported that a stoppage having occurred in Athelstan Road sewer an opening was made and the sewer found to be crushed in for a distance of about 140 feet, the Surveyor proposed this portion of the sewer should be reconstructed. The Borough Surveyor reported that on proceeding with the work of constructing Parnell Road sewer it was found the sewer had to be carried farther down Locton Street and Ruston Street than was expected, owing to a discrepancy in the records of the existing sewers. The Lock Cottages at Duckett's Canal drained into cesspools, and the sewer was 125 feet away, therefore, it was necessary for a sewer to be provided within 100 feet before requiring owners of property to drain into it. I 2 132 The Committee recommended the Council's sewer to be extended to take the drainage of Lock Cottages, and the owners to be required to drain the Cottages into the sewer. The extension of the Bridge Road sewer for a distance of about 90 feet to take the drainage of Messrs. Lamb, Beal and Co.'s premises, was approved by the Council. The Borough Surveyor reported that it was desirable the extension be made in 24-inch pipes, instead of 9-inch as originally proposed, in order that the sewer may form an emergency reservoir in case of any temporary difficulty in connection with the sewage lift. The Borough Surveyor reported that the sewers in Ashton Street and Woolmore Street were in a very defective condition, and that Ashton Street sewer is at too high a level to efficiently drain that street. The Surveyor proposed to reconstruct the sewer in Ashton Street with about 430 feet of 12-inch pipes and Woolmore Street 615 feet of 12-inch pipes, with the necessary manholes and a ventilating shaft in each case. The Committee recommended the Council that the sewers in Ashton Street and Woolmore Street be reconstructeH by the Borough Surveyor in accordance with his plans. The Committee had under consideration the requirement of the London and India Docks Co. that the Council should periodically remove the soil from the cesspit of a privy at the Lockkeeper's Cottage at the Limehouse entrance to the South-West India Dock. The premises were too far from the Council's sewer for the Council to require the Company to connect to it, and the Borough Surveyor advised the Committee it would be much less costly for the Council to incur the necessary expenditure on the work than to carry out the removal of the soil. The Committee recommended the Council that a 6-inch pipe be laid from the Lock-keeper's Cottage at the Limehouse entrance to the South-West India Docks. 133 The Borough Surveyor reported that the extensive sewer works which have been in progress on the Isle of Dogs since January, 1903, were completed on 27th August, 1904. In his report the Borough Surveyor stated: — " The construction of new sewers for the Isle of Dogs was necessitated by the frequent flooding of the basement floors of many premises in Cubitt Town on account of the old sewers having been insufficiently deep to allow of an adequate fall being given to the drain from the premises. In addition to this, however, the old sewers were found to be in a very defective condition, and far from watertight. When it is known that the London County Council have to deal with the whole of the sewage of this area by pumping at Abbey Mills, the importance of this in such a water-logged strata as exists in the Isle of Dogs can be readily imagined. "In October, 1902, the Council approved a scheme presented by me for the construction of new 32-inch by 48-inch egg-shaped sewers composed of bricks and concrete, and 18 inch and 24-inch circular stoneware pipe sewers surrounded by concrete. The estimated cost was £16,820, but this was subsequently increased to £18,520 before the commencement of the work to suit the requirements of the L.C.C. " The work was commenced on 26th January, 1903, at the lowest point, viz., the junction with the Manchester and East Ferry Roads, where the connection with the L.C.C. sewer was made. "During the progress of the work either ballast or clay was encountered in the lower strata until a point 100 feet north of Marshfield Street was reached. There, however, and onwards the lower strata proved to be of running sand on which it was impossible to construct a sound sewer. The depth of this sand varied from 3 feet 6 inches to 5 feet to the solid bottom below the level of the foundation of the sewer, and this, consequently, necessitated either sheet piling and excavation or open piling with square piles. 134 The latter method was adopted, the piles being driven through the loose sand and firmly imbedded in a solid foundation. It was found that as the numerous piles were driven down the water was excluded, and what was previously loose wet sand became a compact mass and formed an excellent bottom to build upon. " A departure had to be made from this method of obtaining a firm bottom on proceeding further on account of the sand containing too large a proportion of silt to allow of its compression. It was, therefore, decided to drive piles as usual and place longitudinally on the top thereof 50 lb. steel rails, fish-plated, bolted and brogged on to the piles. On these rails whea fixed in position 3-inch York stone slabs were placed and on the stone the invert blocks were laid the sewer being then built up in the ordinary fashion. In places the strata of silt ran higher and in such cases the sloppy silt was entirely removed and hardcore and cement concrete substituted. " A length of over 1,000 yards was dealt with either in one or other of the methods above described and many other difficulties overcome. Some idea may be formed of these when I mention that throughout the whole length of the works not more than 200 yards proved sufficiently good to build upon without special treatment. "The total cost of the work has been £21,231 8s. 8d., and the estimate, together with a supplementary estimate of £2,000 put forward during the progress of the work, amounted to £20,520. £11,539 14s. 9d. has been paid in wages, and the Establishment charges included in the above cost amounted to £1,154. " The following information as to the length of sewers, and quantities of material used may be of interest: — Length of brick sewer constructed ... 4,590 feet. Length of 18-inch pipe sewer laid ... 3,336 feet. Length of 24-inch pipe sewer laid ... 950 feet." The Committee considered complaints of offensive emanations 135 from a surface ventilating grating over the sewer in Brabazon Street, and directed the Borough Surveyor! to substitute a ventilating shaft. The Committee considered complaints of nuisance from an open ventilating grating on the L.C.C. sewer embankment at Wick Lane, and requested the London County Council to replace the grating with a ventilating shaft. The Public Health and Housing Committee agreed with the Borough Surveyor that it is desirable that means of access to the sewers should be provided other than the costly method of breaking into them when they required attention and recommended to the Council that 227 manholes be constructed to the Council's sewers in the Bow district. It will be remembered that in my last annual report it was mentioned, the Borough Surveyor very wisely pointed out to the Committee the need for the construction of manholes to existing sewers, to enable the sewers to be properly examined and cleansed. A number of the Council's sewers were without manholes and could only be examined by breaking them open, and, consequently, it was only when a blockage occurred that defects were discovered. The suggestion of the Borough Surveyor as to manholes has been largely carried out throughout the Borough.
