Drainage reconstruction was carried out by the Council at the undermentioned premises, either on account of there being no plans, or the plans not being in order: — 67 to 73, Kerbev Street; 305-307, Roman Road and stables at rear of 74, Usher Road; 88, Upper North Street, and 73 to 77, Augusta Street; 11, Lindale Street; 3 and 4, Grove Villas; 58 and 137 60, Woolmore Street; 52 to 58, Burcham Street; 6 and 8, Parnell Road; 32 and 34, Woolmore Street; 61 to 63, Brunswick Road; 86 and 87, Cadogan Terrace; and 2 to 5, Grove Villas. The St. Pancras Borough Council called attention to the fact (hat when premises within one curtilage are sub-divided into premises within two or more curtilages, the existing drain takes the drainage of more than one premises and may become a sewer. The London County Council was asked to promote a Bill in Parliament to prevent the creation of sewers by the sub-division of premises, and for the imposition of a substantial penalty for creating a sewer by adding drainage beyond the curtilage of any premises. The Committee recommended the Council to support the action of St. Pancras Borough Council in the matter. The following letter was received from the London County Council on the subject of legislation regarding Combined Drainage: — LONDON COUNTY COUNCIL, County Hall, Spring Gardens, S.W., J,tli August, 190If. Sir, The Council has had under consideration a letter, dated 12th February, 1904, from the Council of the Metropolitan Borough of St. Pancras, and also communications from a number of the Metropolitan Borough Councils, suggesting that the Council should promote a Bill in Parliament for the prevention of the creation of sewers by the subdivision of premises, and to provide for a substantial penalty in case of such action. With reference thereto I am directed to remind you that during five sessions of Parliament the Council endeavoured, without success, to obtain legislation with regard to the question of combined drainage; and in October, 1900, it was deemed inadvisable to take further action on the Metropolitan Sewers and Drains Bill, which the Council had promoted. 138 The Council is advised that the prospect of obtaining the powers sought by the Bill has not improved, as the legislation required would constitute an amendment of the Metropolis Management Acts. I am also directed to draw your attention to the reply of Mr. Walter Long, President of the Local Government Board, to the deputation of representatives of the Association of Municipal Corporations and of a conference held in Glasgow. The deputation waited on Mr. Long on 4th February, 1904, and the reply was to the effect that combined drainage was a very complicated matter and that if he could find a satisfactory and practically non-controversial remedy, he would be glad to deal with the difficulty. In these circumstances, the Council suggested to the Council of the Metropolitan Borough of St. Pancras, in reply to its letter of 12th February, 1904, that a joint representation should be made to the Government with a view to the question of combined drainage being dealt with as a public measure. The Borough Council has expressed itself in favour of the suggestion. I have accordingly been directed to enquire whether the Borough Councils, which have expressed themselves in favour of the proposals in the letter, dated 12th February, 1904, from the St. Pancras Metropolitan Borough Council, are prepared to support the suggestion that a joint representation should be made to the Government. Before any such representation is made, however, it would appear necessary that the particular amendments of the law required should be carefully considered, and I am to suggest that the Metropolitan Borough Councils which are moving in the matter should endeavour to come to some arrangement on this point, and should formulate such amendments as appear to them to be desirable. I am, Sir, Your obedient Servant, (Signed) G. L. GOMME, Clerk of the Council. The Town Clerk, Poplar. 139 The matter was referred to the Borough Surveyor and myself, who suggested an amendment of the Metropolis Local Management Acts on the lines of a provision obtained by the West Ham Corporation in their Act of 1898, Section 42, which enacts:— 42. "In and for the purposes of Section 41 of the Act of ''1893, the word ' drain' shall be deemed to include any sewer "or drain whether constructed before or after the passing of "this Act with which two or more houses or premises (whether "belonging to the same or different owners) are at the date of " the passing of this Act or may at any time thereafter be con"nected or which is used or capable of being or intended to be ''used for the conveyance of the drainage of such houses or " buildings directly or by means of any other sewer or drain to " any public sewer situate under a street repairable by the in" habitants at large but shall not include any sewer which has "been constructed to the satisfaction of the Corporation under "Section 152 of the Public Health Act, 1875, or any sewer ''which has been constructed by the Corporation for the effectual "drainage of the Borough." Section 41 of the West Ham Corporation Act, 1893, is practically the same as Sections 82, 83, 84 and 85 of the Metropolis Local Management Act, 1855, and Section 64 of the Metropolis Local Management Act, 1862. The effect of this provision is that all sewers and drains, other than public sewers constructed by or taken over by the Corporation are maintainable by the owners. The Public Health and Housing Committee being of opinion the amendment of the Metropolis Local Management Acts on similar lines is desirable, and that a joint representation should be made to the Government with a view to the introduction of a public bill dealing with the matter, the Committee recommended the Council to concur in this opinion, and the London County Council be so informed. 140 BY-LAWS MADE BY THE LONDON COUNTY COUNCIL UNDER SECTION 39 (I.) OF THE PUBLIC HEALTH (LONDON) ACT, 1891, WITH RESPECT TO WATER CLOSETS, EARTH CLOSETS, PRIVIES, AND RECEPTACLES FOR DUNG. After the judgment of the High Court (King's Bench Division) on appeal from decisions of Police Magistrates in connection with enforcement of the by-laws relating to Houses Let in Lodgings (see page 129), the London County Council proposed to amend By-law 26, made by the1 Council under Section 39 (I.) of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891. The London County Council after consultation with the Local Government Board submitted the amended by-laws to the Local Sanitary Authorities. The Public Health Committee recommended the Council to approve of the amended by-laws, and the seal of the London County Council was affixed on the 10th November, 1904, to the following by-laws, which were allowed by the Local Government Board on the 31st January, 1905. 1.—The occupier of any premises shall cause every water closet belonging to such premises to be thoroughly cleansed from time to time as often as may be necessary for the purpose of keeping such water closet in a cleanly condition. The occupier of any premises shall once at least in every week cause every receptacle for dung belonging to such premises to be emptied and thoroughly cleansed. Provided that where two or more lodgers in a lodging-house are entitled to the use in common of any water closet or receptacle for dung the landlord shall cause such water closet to be cleansed or receptacle for dung to be emptied and cleansed as aforesaid. The owner of any lodging-house shall, subject to the provision 141 hereinafter specified provide and maintain in connection with such house water closet earth closet or privy accommodation in the proportion of not less than one water closet earth closet or privy for every 12 inmates of such house. Provided that proceedings shall not he taken against the owner of any lodging-house for an offence against the last mentioned requirement of this by-law unless and until the owner after service upon him of a notice in writing by the sanitary authority requiring him within such reasonable time as is specified in the notice to comply with the by-law has failed to comply with the by-law within the time so specified. For the purposes of this by-law a "lodging-house" means a house or part of a house which is let in lodgings or occupied by members of more than one family. "Landlord" in relation to a house or part of a house which is let in lodgings or occupied by members of more than one family means the person (whatever may be the nature or extent of his interest) by whom or on' whose behalf such house or part of a house is let in lodgings; or for occupation by members of more thai> one family or who for the time being receives or is entitled to receive the profits arising from such letting. "Lodger," in relation to a house or part of a house which is let in lodgings or occupied by members of more than one family means a person to whom any room or rooms in such house or part of a house may have been let as a lodging or for his use or occupation. Nothing in this by-law shall extend to any common lodging-house, Penalties. 2.—Every person who shall offend against the foregoing by-law shall be liable for every such offence to a penalty of five pounds and in the case of a continuing offence to a further penalty of forty shillings for each day after written notice of the offence from the sanitary authority. 142 Provided nevertheless that the court before whom any complaint may be made or any proceedings may be taken in respect of any such offence may if they think fit adjudge the payment as a penalty of any sum less than the full amount of the penalty imposed by this by-law. Repeal of By-laws. 3.—From and after the date of the confirmation of these by-laws the by-law numbered 26 (twenty-six) in the by-laws relating to water closets earth closets privies ash pits cesspools and receptacles for dung and the proper accessories thereof in connection with buildings which were made by the London County Council on the twentysecond day of June in the year One thousand eight hundred and ninety-three and were confirmed by the Local Government Board on the twenty-eighth day of June in the year One thousand eight hundred and ninety-three, shall be repealed. OFFENSIVE TRADES. A letter was received from the London County Council stating the Local Government Board had confirmed the Order made by the Council on 29th March, declaring the business of a dresser of fish skins to be an offensive business within the meaning of Section 19 (1) (b) of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891. At the present time there is no known dresser of fish skins within the Borough of Poplar. The Public Health and Housing Committee were informed that the Public Health Committee of the London County Council were of opinion that it is desirable the by-laws made by the Council in relation to offensive trades should be amended, and they had accordingly caused draft amended by-laws to be prepared. The most important amendment which it was proposed to make is to require that, in the case of every business, all offensive processes be conducted in a closed chamber, provision being moreover made for the adequate ventilation of such chamber and the destruction of offensive vapours. 143 The amended draft by-laws also differ from the existing by-laws in the following, among other, respects:— For the purpose of receiving and conveying away all scraps, litter, refuse, and residues, there is to be provided in all cases a sufficient number of vessels or receptacles, furnished with tight and close-fitting covers. All material emitting any offensive or noxious effluvium, vapour or gas when delivered on to the premises across a public footway is required to be conveyed in vessels properly constructed and covered. In all cases where a closed chamber is to be provided, the cleansing of the floor of every such chamber and such part of the walls thereof as is covered with hard, smooth, and impervious material is required at the close of each working day; and all vessels, receptacles, etc., are to be kept clean so as to prevent any offensive smell. A copy of the by-laws and a plan of the premises is to be displayed in a conspicuous position. Drains on all premises are to be maintained in good order and efficient action. In some instances a requirement is made that all liquid refuse before being discharged into any drain shall be treated in such manner as to prevent nuisance. In the by-laws relating to the business of a knacker, a provision has been inserted for the prevention of over-crowding of animals, and the cleansing of streets after the slaughter of horses by a licensed knacker. A provision that the parts of walls and ceilings not covered with hard, smooth, and impervious materials shall be lime-washed between the first and tenth days of the months of March, June, September, and December has been inserted in each set of by-laws. 144 It was reported to the Committee that the proposed amended by-laws are necessary to enable nuisances arising from offensive businesses to be adequately dealt with, and the Committee recommended the Council to approve the draft amended by-laws. The premises where offensive trades may be carried on within the Borough of Poplar are:— Yard attached to 39, White Post Lane 277, Roman Road Tripe Boilers. East London Soap Works Soap Boilers. 241, Old Ford Road 37, White Post Lane Fat Melters. 112, White Post Lane 31, White Post Lane Fat and Bone Webbs, Yeo Street Wickens, Pease & Co., Glaucus Street Boilers. Complaints were received respecting the obnoxious smells arising from the Sheppy Glue and Chemical Works, White Post Lane. The Company was communicated with and a very much larger fan was fixed to extract the fumes from the boiling house and drive them through the furnaces. No further complaints were received after this alteration. The Company was also informed that the tubs for collecting fat should be abolished, as the wood had become infiltrated, and properly covered iron receptacles ought to be substituted. Iron receptacles were obtained. REMOVAL OF OFFENSIVE TRADE AND OTHER REFUSE. Suggested Amendment of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891. In a circular letter dated 27th February, 1903, the London County Council pointed out that no provision exists which suggests that the Sanitary Authority shall systematically collect trade refuse, but they must remove any particular accumulation on the requisition of the owner at his expense. 145 Under date 15th April a letter was received from the London County Council referring to their previous circular letter. The letter stated that this proposal was made on account of resolutions adopted by the Conference of London Sanitary Authorities held at the County Hall on the 29th June and 7th November, 1900. From replies received to the circular letter oh the 27th February, 1903, it appeared that a large number of Sanitary Authorities were bpposed to the proposal that it should be their duty to collect trade refuse, and with a view of meeting their objection, clauses had been re-drafted so as to enable, but not to compel Sanitary Authorities to undertake any such collection. The Public Health and Housing Committee considered it desirable Borough Councils should have power to arrange for the systematic removal of offensive trade refuse, and recommended the Council to approve these suggested clauses of the London County Council, which were as follows:— The Sanitary Authority if they think fit may:— 1. (a) Employ a sufficient number of scavengers, or contract with any scavengers, whether a company or individuals, for collecting and removing at proper periods, of which sufficient notice shall be given, all trade refuse of an offensive or noxious nature from any premises within their district. (b) Where the Sanitary Authority, in pursuance of the power hereby conferred, has undertaken the collection of such refuse and such refuse is not removed from any premises in the district at the period so fixed as aforesaid, and the owner or occupier serves on the Sanitary Authority a written notice requiring removal of such refuse, the Sanitary Authority shall comply with such notice within 48 hours after that service, exclusive of Sundays and public holidays. If a Sanitary Authority fail without reasonable cause to comply with such notice they shall be liable on conviction by a court of summary jurisdiction to a fine not exceeding twenty pounds. k 146 2. Offensive or noxious trade refuse shall mean all trade refuse in such a condition as to be or to be liable to become offensive or noxious. If any dispute or difference of opinion arises between the owner or occupier and the Sanitary Authority as to what is to be considered as offensive or noxious trade refuse, a petty sessional court, on complaint made by either party, may by order determine whether the subject matter of dispute is offensive or noxious trade refuse, and the decision of that court shall be final. 3. The owner or occupier of any premises from which such refuse is removed shall pay to the Sanitary Authority a reasonable sum, either by way of annual payment or in respect of each such removal, and such sum in case of dispute shall be settled by the order of a petty sessional court. Complaint was made respecting the nuisance arising from the removal of fish offal; the communication alleging the complaint was laid before the Committee. It was pointed out to the Committee a nuisance must always arise whilst the offal is being removed in open pails from premises and is being shot into the receptacle or cart. The nuisance can only be prevented by having the offal stored in air-tight covered pails or receptacles, and these pails or receptacles removed from premises and placed on a trolley without their covers Being disturbed. No doubt if the strict letter of the London County Council bylaw be enforced some such mode of removal could be made compulsorily. The by-law states:— "Every person who shall convey any house, trade or street "refuse across or along any footway, pavement or carriageway "shall use a suitable receptacle, cart, carriage or other means of "conveyance properly constructed so as to prevent the escape "of the contents thereof, and in case of offensive refuse so "covered as to prevent any nuisance therefrom. . . '' 147 As the Council had already approved the proposal of the London County Council that legislation should be promoted enabling Borough Councils to undertake by contract or otherwise systematic collection of offensive refuse, the Committee, meanwhile, directed that) such steps should be taken as may be possible to minimise the nuisance. Mr. Boyce, Sanitary Inspector Bow West Division, reported:— "On the 11th July at 5.15 p.m. my attention was drawn by the Medical Officer of Health to a pair-horse van loaded with bags containing stinking fat and bones, at rest outside a coffee-shop in the Bow Road." Summonses were applied for. It was reported to the Committee that there is an increase in the nuisance from the removal of offensive or noxious matter outside the prescribed hours, and in vehicles not properly constructed or covered. The vehicles conveying offensive or noxious matter pass through the district at all hours and are driven at such a pace That the officers of the Sanitary Authority cannot cause the drivers to stop in order to obtain their names and addresses, and those of their employers. The drivers can only be questioned should they happen to pull up on their journey. When the by-laws were first passed in June, 1903, the Police used to communicate with the Local Authority whose duty it is to prosecute, and the Police giving the necessary evidence. A case was heard at the Thames Police Court upon information given by the Police, but the Magistrate dismissed the summons as he did not consider the matter removed was of an offensive nature, and since the dismissal of this case the Police have taken no active steps in assisting the Poplar Sanitary Authority. k 2 148 In dismissing the case mentioned above, the Magistrate stated he did not consider matter could be considered offensive if in order to obtain the effluvia it was necessary to mount the vehicle conveying it. It was suggested to the Committee that the Commissioner of Police be communicated with, asking him to assist the Sanitary Authority by the aid of the mounted patrols in enforcing the bylaws made by the London County Council in respect of the removal by road of offensive or noxious matter through the Poplar District. The Committee directed the Commissioner of Police be communicated with. REMOVAL OF DEAD HORSES. The London County Council proposed to make, under Section 54 of the London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1903, a by-law as follows:— Any person who shall convey the carcase of a dead horse through or along a public street in the County of London shall use for such purpose a vehicle constructed and closed in such manner that such carcase shall be completely concealed from public view, and the body of such vehicle shall be made of or lined with impervious material so as to prevent any liquid or discharge from the said carcase escaping from such vehicle. He shall so convey such carcase along the public streets with all reasonable dispatch, and so as to prevent any nuisance arising therefrom. Any person who shall offend against the foregoing by-law shall be liable for each breach of such by-law to a penalty not exceeding forty shillings. This by-law shall not extend to the City of London. 149 The Committee recommended the Council to approve the proposed by-law. The by-law was made by the London County Council on the 11th of October, and confirmed whilst this report is being written, 011 the 31st of January, 1903, and approved by the Local Government Board on the 21st of February. 1905. Section 55 of the London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1903, imposes the duty of enforcing the by-law upon the Metropolitan Borough Councils. CONFERENCE ON THE ADMINISTRATION OF THE PUBLIC HEALTH (LONDON) ACT, 1891. The London County Council were of opinion that it would be an advantage for representatives of the Metropolitan Borough Councils to meet and exchange views as to the results of the experience they had gained in the administration of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891; and to discuss any general principles which such experience showed it was desirable to adopt with a view to the provisions of the Act being equally and uniformly enforced in the several sanitary districts of London. With this object a conference was held at the County Hall, on Thursday, the 7th July, at 11 a.m. Together with Councillors Sambridge and West and the Town Clerk, I attended a conference of representatives of the London Sanitary Authorities at the County Hall, Spring Gardens. The conference was convened on the 7th July by the London County Council, and was adjourned after a lengthy meeting to the 18th July. The following are the resolutions adopted by the conference on 7th July, 1904:— housing. Homes let in Lodgings. (6) That the efficient administration of Section 94 of the Public 150 Health (London) Act, 1891, relating to the registration and regulation of houses let in lodgings is a matter of extreme importance to the public health of London. (7) That it is advisable that in by-laws made in respect of houses let in lodgings there should be no rental limit of exemption, but that they should be so framed as to apply only to such houses as are registered by the sanitary authority. (8) That the efficient enforcement of by-laws relating to houses let in lodgings is the most effective method of dealing with overcrowding, but that inspections should from time to time be made during the early hours of the morning at other houses where overcrowding within the meaning of Section (2), (1), (e) of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, is suspected. Underground Rooms. (9) That a single angle of light and air to underground dwelling rooms should be adopted in the enforcing of Section 96 of the Public Health (London) Act. (10) That this conference is of opinion that no underground room not complying with the conditions laid down by Section 96 of the Public Health Act, 1891, should be used for sleeping. Water Step ply. (11) That a draw-off tap from the rising main and a sink should be provided on even- floor of a tenement house. Factories and Workshops. (12) That it is desirable that the sanitary authorities in enforcing Section 38 of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, should adopt the following standards: — I. In factories and workshops where females are employed or in attendance there shall be one sanitary convenience for every 25 151 females, provided that in factories or workshops where the number of females employed or in attendance does not exceed 75 the following shall be the standard: — One sanitary convenience where no more than 15 females are employed or in attendance. Two sanitary conveniences where from 15 to 40 females are employed or in attendance. Three sanitary conveniences where from 40 to 75 females are employed or in attendance. II. In factories or workshops where males are employed or in attendance there shall be one sanitary convenience for every 25 males; provided that— (a) In factories or workshops where the number of males employed or in attendance does not exceed 50, and sufficient urinal accommodation is also provided there shall be one sanitary convenience where no more than 20 males are employed or in attendance, and two sanitary conveniences where from 20 to 50 males are employed or in attendance. (b) In factories or workshops where the number of males employed or in attendance exceeds 100, and sufficient urinal accommodation is also provided, it shall be sufficient if there is one sanitary convenience for every 25 males up to the first 100, and one for every 40 after. (c) In factories or workshops where the number of males employed or in attendance exceeds 500, and the district inspector of factories certifies in writing that, by means of a check system, or otherwise, proper supervision and control in regard to the use of the conveniences are exercised by officers specially appointed for that purpose, it shall be sufficient if one sanitary convenience is provided for every 60 males, in addition to sufficient urinal accommodation. Any certificate given by an inspector shall be kept attached to the general register, and shall be liable at any time to be revoked by notice in writing from the inspector. (In 152 calculating the number of conveniences required, any odd number of persons less than 15, 20, 25, 40, or 60, as the case may be, shall be reckoned as 15, 20, 25, 40, or 60.) Shops. (13) That it is desirable that power should be given to local authorities to inspect the sleeping accommodation in shops where the assistants are boarded in. food. Premises where Food is prepared for Sale. (14) That the London County Council be requested to insert in their General Powers Bill for the Session of 1905 a clause enabling them to make by-laws for the regulation of the structure of premises where food is prepared for sale and that no such premises should be used unless certified by the Borough Council to be suitable for the purpose. (15) That the London County Council be requested to insert their General Powers Bill for the Session of 1905 a clause enabling them to make by-laws for the regulation of the conduct of the business carried on in premises where food is prepared for sale, such by-laws to be conferred by the Borough Councils. Abolition of Private Slaughter-houses. (16) That the time has arrived when private slaughter-houses should cease to exist in London. Inspection of Meat killed in Private Slaughter-houses. (17) That it is desirable that Borough Councils should endeavour to have all meat that is killed inspected. Inspection of Slaughter-houses, Cowhouses, Dairies, Milkshops and Milk-stores. (18) That slaughter-houses, cow-houses, dairies, milk shops and milk stores should be frequently inspected. 153 Registration, of Milk Shops. (19) That the London County Council be requested to take powers to enable the Metropolitan Borough Councils to refuse registration of premises as milk shops where, in their opinion, the premises are unfit for the purpose. Sale of Milk. (20) That the London County Council be requested to introduce legislation providing for the penalising of persons selling filthy milk in London. Municipal Milk Depots. (21) That, having regard to the declining birth rate and the large infantile mortality rate of the Metropolis, it is desirable that the Metropolitan Borough Councils should be empowered, at their discretion, to provide in their respective districts depots for the preparation and sale of sterilised and humanised milk for the food of infants, and that the London County Council be requested to insert a clause in their General Powers Bill for the Session of 1905 to confer such power upon the said Borough Councils. Sale of Food and Drugs Acts. (22) (a) That, in the opinion of this Conference, and provisions of Section 25 of the Food and Drugs Act, 1875, and of Section 20 of the Food and Drugs Act, 1899. operate to the prejudice of the purchaser and of public health, and should be repealed, (b) That the London County Council be requested to make a communication, conveying the resolution of the Conference to the President of the Local Government Board, with a view of his promoting legislation for this purpose. (23) That the Local Government Board and the Board of Agriculture be asked to take such steps as may be necessary in order that a limit may be fixed as to the amount of boracic acid—that is free or combined boracic acid—or other preservatives which may be mixed with food other than milk. 154 (24) That every registered milk seller in a Borough shall have his milk tested frequently. I he following are the resolutions adopted by the Conference on 18th July, 1904:— Cisterns. (25) That, in cases where there is a constant water supply, cisterns should only be used in connection with hot water services and sanitary conveniences. prevention of disease. Disinfection. (26) That this Conference is of opinion that disinfection should be carried out by the responsible officers of the sanitary authority without the intervention of a contractor. (27) That the London County Council be asked to take measures to amend the law with regard to disinfection, so that disinfection should be carried out by the responsible officers of the sanitary authority without the intervention of a contractor, and that in all cases the disinfection should be to the satisfaction of the medical officer of health. Provision of Laundry. (28) That the provision of a laundry for use in connection with the disinfection of infected articles is essential. Provision of Permanent Shelter Accommodation. (29) That at least one permanent shelter for temporary use should be provided in each borough for the accommodation of persons during the disinfection of their homes. (30) That the word "accommodation" mentioned in Section 60, sub-Section 4 of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, should, if and when necessary, include the expense of the supply of food. 155 Precautionary Measures against Phthisis. (31) That the system of voluntary notification of phthisis is desirable, and should be made general throughout London. (32) That the sanitary authorities should disinfect the premises in which a person suffering from phthisis has died, or from which such a patient has removed or has been removed. Hospital Accommodation. (33) That it is desirable to make provision of sanatoria for the isolation and treatment of persons suffering from consumption, for the Metropolis as a whole, and under one authority; and that, in the opinion of this Conference, the Metropolitan Asylums Board should be such authority. (34) That the foregoing resolution be sent to the Local Government Board with the request that they give the matter their immediate consideration. Offensive Trades. (35) That the business of a marine store dealer should be added to the list of offensive businesses specified in Section 19 of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891. (3G) That the business of a fried fish shop keeper should be added to the list of offensive businesses specified in Section 19 of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891. (37) That the London County Council be requested to make an order to carry out these resolutions. House Refuse. (38) That this Conference is of opinion that dust collection should be carried out by the responsible officers of the sanitary authority without the intervention of a contractor. 156 (39) That the daily removal of house refuse in suitable districts is advisable. (40) That improved dust carts should be adopted so covered as to minimise annoyance caused to passers-by during collection of house refuse, especially in crowded thoroughfares. (41) That the method of destruction of house refuse by lire should be universally adopted in London. Collection of Fish Offal and other Offensive Trade Refuse. (42) That the collection of fish offal and other offensive trade refuse should be undertaken by the sanitary authorities at the expense of the producers. sanitary inspection. (43) That female inspectors should be employed in each Borough. miscellaneous. Municipal Creches. (44) That, having regard to the declining birth-rate and the large infantile mortality rate of the metropolis, it is desirable that the Metropolitan Borough Councils should be empowered at their discretion to establish creches for the reception of young children during the hours their mothers are employed in work away from home, and to make reasonable charges for the accommodation so provided and for the food of the children, and that the London County Council be requested to insert in their General Powers Bill for the Session of 1905 a clause to confer such power upon the said Borough Councils. Smoke Nuisances. (45) That in the opinion of this Conference it is desirable that the provisions of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, as to nuisances arising from the emission of smoke, should be applied to property in the possession of the Crown. 157 Metropolitan Ambulance Service. (46) That the Conference, taking note that the Metropolitan Asylums Board are prepared to extend the operations of their ambulance service so as to include the transport of medical, surgical, and mental cases, respectfully requests the Local Government Board to obtain for the Managers the requisite legal authority to enable them to do so. (47) That the Conference desires to express the opinion that should the London County Council not be able to provide for a street ambulance service the duty should be undertaken by the Metropolitan Asylums Board. Application of Acta to Government Property. (48) That all provisions of the Public Health and Sanitary Acts should apply to Government property. LONDON COUNTY COUNCIL (GENERAL POWERS) ACT, 1904. I attended the Parliamentary Committee of the House of Commons, and gave evidence in respect of the London County Council (General Powers) Bill, Part IV. The Act was passed on the 15th August. Section 19.—Enables Sanitary Authorities to require the cleansing or destruction of articles of clothing which are in a filthy, dangerous or unwholesome condition. Section 20.—Enables Sanitary Authorities to require the owners of premises to strip the walls and cleanse any room or rooms infested with vermin, and enables owners to recover the cost of the work from tenants in certain cases. Section 22.—Enables Sanitary Authorities to require the reconstruction or removal of urinals or other conveniences opening on to streets and constructed before the passing of the Public Health 158 (London) Act, 1891, and so placed as to be a nuisance or offensive to public decency. Section 23.—Enables Sanitary Authorities to require the removal of fixed dust receptacles after movable receptacles have been provided. Section 48.—Amends Part VIII. of the London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1902, so as to make penalties against ice cream manufacturers and vendors payable to the Metropolitan Borough Councils. Shortly after the passing of the Act, the owners of 18 publichouses and beer-houses were called upon to provide doors to the urinals attached to their premises. The owner of the "Wades Arms" Public House, Jeremiah Street, was called upon to remove the existing convenience, which was so situated as to be offensive to public decency. A notice was served upon an owner of a house in Bow under Section 20, as the premises were infested with bugs. The work in all the bove instances was satisfactorily carried out. WATER SUPPLIES TO SCHOOLS, In the annual report for 1903 it was stated the Committee had submitted to the School Board for London ai report on the drinking water arrangements at the Schools in the Borough, suggesting that the water fountains should be placed in or near the class-rooms, under the immediate supervision of the teachers, and that the children should be educated up to the necessity for rinsing the drinking cups before using them. A reply was received early this year that the Works Committee of the School Board of London were of opinion the necessity for rinsing the drinking cups before use is among the matters of detail 159 which should be taught in the Schools, and directed the attention of the School Management Committee to the subject. Apart from this, the School Board Committee did not consider any alterations in the present drinking arrangements were necessary. The public Health and Housing Committee directed that a copy of the report and recommendations on the subject be sent to the London County Council. RAILWAY STATIONS. Inspection of Sanitary Conveniences. The sanitary conveniencesi of the railway stations throughout the Borough were inspected during the year, the sanitary offices for gentlemen by the Sanitary Inspectors, and the sanitary offices in the ladies' waiting rooms by Miss Tattersall. A large amount of sanitary work was executed to remedy the defects revealed. The full reports in detail of these defects were presented to the Public Health and Housing Committee, and need not be again repeated. The railway stations inspected were: — Millwall Docks Station (M.E.R.). Millwall Junction Station (G.E.R.). West India Docks Station (G.E.R.). North Greenwich Station (M.E.R.). Poplar Station (G.E.R.). Poplar Station (N.L.R.). South Bromley Station (N.L.R.). Bromley Station (L.T. and S.R.). Bow Station (N.L.R.). Old Ford (N.L.R.). Bow Road Station (G.E.R.). The Blackwall Railway Station (G.E.R.) is within the jurisdiction of the London Port Sanitary Authority, but the offices for ladies were inspected by Miss Tattersall and found in fair condition. 160 The South Dock Railway Station (M.E.R.) is also within the jurisdiction of the London Port Sanitary Authority and joint inspection was made with a Sanitary Inspector of that Authority. There is a new railway station, namely Bow Road Station (Met. and L.T. and S.R.), recently built, there was no need to inspect this station. CUSTOMS AND INLAND REVENUE ACT, 1890. Cap. 8, Sect 26 (2). SANITARY INSPECTORS' WORK, HOUSE TO HOUSE INSPECTION, OVERCROWDING, &c., AND POLICE COURT PROCEEDINGS. After a short illness, Mr. F. J. Anthony, Sanitary Inspector and Inspector under the Food and Drugs Acts, and Canal Boat Inspector for the Bromley North Division, died on the 24th February. Mr. Anthony had been in the service of the Poplar Sanitary Authority since 1880, a period of 24 years. He first acted as Disinfector, and having qualified himself by passing the 161 tion of the Sanitary Institute, he was appointed Sanitary Inspector to the Bromley North Division in June, 1893. During his 24 years' service, Mr. Anthony had also charge of the Infectious Disease Mortuary belonging to the Council in Wellington Road. Mr. Anthony was a most trustworthy, reliable and fearless officer, and his death was very much regretted by everybody who knew him. Mr. H. J. Langley (Temporary Inspector, Houses Let in Lodgings) was appointed to carry out the duties hitherto carried out by the late Mr. F. J. Anthony, Sanitary Inspector (Bromley North Division), and Mr. Wm. Johnson was appointed to undertake the work which had been carried out by Mr. Langley. The journals of the Sanitary Inspectors were examined from the date of the coming into operation of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, and lists were compiled (which involved a large amount of work) of houses not inspected, and also those inspected with the number of inspections made. Lists of houses not inspected were given to the Inspectors with instructions to inspect such premises as soon as possible. Through this proceeding many defective drainage systems have been discovered. See Inspectors' Reports, pages 189, 195, 199 and 213. Where W.C. pans and traps are fixed without notice being given under Clause 14 of the London County Council By-laws, Section 39 (1) of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, the builder is written to asking him to open the ground, otherwise the matter will be reported to the Public Health and Housing Committee in order that legal proceedings may be instituted against him for infringing the by-law. This mode of procedure prevents good work being constructed on to defective work. Whenever drainage work has been found to have been done and covered up without inspection, if the builder will not open the ground, the work is exposed by the Sanitary Authority, and such a line of action ensures sound work. Cruelty to Children (Prevention) Act, 1904. "This statute is, in the main, a consolidating Act. It re-enacts, "almost verbatim, the provisions of the Prevention of Cruelty to "Children Act, 1894, which was itself a consolidating and amending "statute, and incorporates the amendments introduced by the "Employment of Children Act, 1903. Among the few new pro"visions the following may be mentioned. The offences enumerated "in the schedule, for the commission of which against a child the "provisions of this statute for the detention and disposal of a "child may be brought into operation, are considerably extended, "so as to include assault and battery, attempt to commit an in"famous crime, and manslaughter. Exhibiting a child for profit "is made a special offence. A parent or guardian is no longer "excluded from the clause which renders penal the training of a "child for any dangerous performance or exhibition. Boards of "Guardians are specially empowered to subscribe towards any "association for the prevention of cruelty to children."—(Patersons Practical Statutes.) Shop Hours Act, 1904. "This statute carries one stage further the legislation affecting "shops, which began in 1902. By the Shop Hours Act of that "year (55 and 56 Vict. c. 62) it was enacted that no young "person (under the age of eighteen) should be employed in a "shop for more than seventy-four hours in one week, and county "and borough councils were empowered (but not compelled) to "appoint inspectors for carrying the Act into execution. By an "amending Act of 1893 (56 and 57 Vict. c. 67), power was given "to defray the expenses incurred; and by another amending Act "of 1895 (58 Vict. c. 5), a penalty was imposed upon a shop"keeper who failed to keep a required notice exhibited. By an "Act of 1899 (62 and 63 Vict. c. 21), seats are required to be "provided for female assistants in retail shops. It will be observed "that all these statutes are obligatory, but refer only to young "persons or women. The present statute, which is expressly "required to be read with them, is permissive, and applies to all "shops and all persons in them. It empowers a local authority "(a county or borough council, or the council of an urban district "with a population of more than 20,000), with the sanction of the "central authority (the Home Secretary) to make an order closing "shops for retail trade for certain hours and under certain condi- 168 "tions. The hour for closing may not be earlier than 7 p.m. "except for one day in the week, when it may be as early as "1 p.m. The order may apply to all shops, or to shops of a "specified class, though a list of trades or businesses (in the "schedule) is expressly exempted. It may apply to the whole or "any part of the area under the local authority. But such a "closing order can only be made when the local authority is "satisfied that it has the approval of at least two-thirds in number "of the occupiers of shops affected. When confirmed by the Home "Secretary, the closing order must be laid before Parliament, and "may be annulled by the Crown on an address from either House. "It may also> be cancelled by the Home Secretary on the appli"cation of the local authority, who are required to make such "application if they are satisfied that a majority of occupiers of "shops affected are opposed to the continuance of the order. The "Home Secretary is empowered to make regulations for carrying "the Act into effect, and also to hold local enquiries."—(Patersons Practical Statutes.) Agreeably to my feelings I cannot close this report without thanking Mr. Shillinglaw, the principal Clerk in the Town Clerk's Department for his kind assistance which at all times he has so willingly rendered me, adding efficiency to the working of the Public Health Department. Mr. Shillinglaw acts as Clerk to the Public Health and Housing Committee, and has recorded the resolutions, recommendations and reports of the Committee which have been quoted in this report. I am, Sir and Gentlemen, Your obedient Servant, Member of the Royal College of Surgeons of England, and Licentiate of the Royal Colleqe of Physicians of Edinburgh ; Diplomate in Public Health, Royal College of Physicians and Surgeons, England ; Fellow and Member of the Council of the Royal Institute of Public Health; Member of the Lister Institute of Preventive Medicine ; Medical Officer of Health. Sir,— I beg to lay before you my fifteenth Annual Summary of the Sanitary work carried out in my Division during the year ending 31st December, 1904, as follows: — Inspections on complaints of nuisances 986 Do. on notifications of infectious diseases 296 Do. on house-to-house visitation 232 Do. Canal Boats 25 Re-Inspections 2,720 Making a total of 4,259 visits Details and results of such inspections from time to time have been entered in the book kept for that purpose, as prescribed by the Public Health (London) Act, 1891. Of the total number (1,514 houses and other premises) of primary inspections made, I found that 37.9 per cent of the premises thus inspected various works of sanitary amendment were needed to be carried out—requiring the service of 575 written intimations, 393 statutory, and 63 final notices. In many of these cases on the service of the usual written intimations, which in all cases precedes the service of statutory notices, the required works were executed by the respective persons liable, but in 2 cases it was found necessary to have recourse to legal proceedings, necessitating attendances at the police-court to ensure enforcement. The following combined systems of drainage or mains have been relaid by the Borough Council as Public Sewers: — 87, Cadogan Terrace. House-to-House Inspection. The house-to-house inspection comprised the whole of the houses in the under-mentioned streets, viz.: — Brewery Yard. Beachy Road. Atley Road. Spring Street. Remus Road. Gandy Court. Walters Court. Smeed Road. Stour Road. And sections of: — Monier Road. Wyke Road. Maverton Road. Bakers Alley. Bow Road. Rippoth Road. Kelday Road. Bream Street. Wansbeck Road. Bakehouses. The bakehouses throughout the district have been periodically visited, and the conditions in some instances necessitating service of intimation for dirty walls and flooring, accumulations of sweepings or ashes beneath the troughs, and the half-yearly cleansing not carried out at the stated periods. With two exceptions the bakehouses of the district are conducted on the ground level. 179 Dairies, Milkshops and Offensive Businesses. The milkshops and offensive businesses in the district have been periodically visited; an intimation in the case of offensive business being sufficient to secure amendment. In one or two instances, milkshops have changed hands without the incoming tenant registering, through ignorance of existence of regulations. On their attention being directed to infringement, they immediately registered. In three instances, attention has been drawn to rusty and broken condition of measures, resulting in the condition being forthwith remedied. The conditions of storage vessels, and utensils generally have been very satisfactory. Two vendors have permanently discontinued sale of milk, and three shops have been newly added to the register. In the case of a fat melters from which odoursi escaped periodically, a larger extracting fan was recommended to be employed. The fan was accordingly provided, and the nuisance apparently abated at once. At the Thames Police Court on the 28th October, a bone boiler was fined 20s. and 23s. costs for allowing offensive matter to be carried through the district, and not contained in proper vessels, during prohibited hours. The carman was at the same time fined 1s. and 2s. costs in respect of the same offence. For a similar offence another firm was summoned, the offenders were fined 20s. and 23s. costs. Canal Boats. Ninety-four boats have been examined; on three of the registered boats an infringement of the Acts or regulations was discovered, as follows: — " Bride," Reg. No. 228, London.—The cabin was very damp M 2 180 and dirty. Cabin top leaking and rotten, and saturated with moisture. No glass in skylight. Name of boat on after combing, and Reg. No. on forward combing. Whole of hull badly worn and in rotten and dilapilated condition. At the Thames Police Court on 25th April, the owner was fined 5s. and 2s. costs for improper marking, and 20s. with 23s. costs for rotton and damp condition of boat. "Northampton," Reg. No. 815, Birmingham.—Fore cabin occupied by extra hand. Cabin badly ventilated, defective stove leaking into cabin. Cabin improperly used for storing sacks of corn for towing horse. Owner and Registration Authority communicated with. "Hart," Reg. No. 143, Uxbridge.—After cabin occupied by man, wife, and sister of wife, aged 13 years, contrary to Act. Owner and Registration Authority communicated with. Factory and Workshop Inspections. Nuisances arising within the several factories and workshops have been satisfactorily dealt with. The whole of the ascertained workshops have been inspected and found, in most cases, to be in a cleanly condition. Where necessary intimations were served for cleansing and the work was immediately carried out. Smoke Nuisances. The smoke nuisances arising within and without the district have received much attention. It has been found necessary to issue four summons. At the West Ham Police Court on 27th January, an abatement order for 14 days and a prohibitive order with a penalty of £5 to include costs was obtained against the Saw Mills Co., Ltd., of Cooks Road, Stratford. 181 At the same Court, abatement and prohibitive orders with £1 8s. 6d. costs was obtained against Messrs. Gould Bros, of Stratford. At the Thames Police Court on 25th April.—The owner, of a traction engine which had emitted dense black smoke in Bow Road, was fined £5 and 23s. costs. On 15th September, the Road Breaking Co., Ltd., of Broadway Chambers, Westminster, was fined £3 and 23s. costs for emitting dense black smoke from a traction engine in Bow Road. Since the hearing of these summonses a notable improvement has been perceptible in the reduction of smoke issuing from traction engines passing through the district. Meat and Food Inspection. The food supplies within the district have been vigilantly watched. On 4th March.—A large box of herrings was found unfit for food and destroyed. On 27th June.—A pad of skate was examined and found unfit for food. It was destroyed by owner, and a certificate given as to its condition. On 11th July.—About half a bushel of whelks was examined and found unfit for food. Destroyed by owner and a certificate of condition given. On 4th August.—Two trunks of haddocks were found unfit for food. Destroyed by owner and certificate of unfitness given. On 5th August.—A trunk of haddocks was examined and found to be unsound. A certificate of its condition was given, and the fish was destroyed by owner. 182 On 6th August.—A pad of plaice was examined and found to be unfit for food. Certificate as to condition was given and the fish was destroyed by owner. On 27th August.—Two barrels of Canadian boned beef were examined and found to be totally unfit for food. The meat was destroyed. On 15th September.—A trunk of fish was examined and found unfit for food. The fish was destroyed, and a certificate of unfitness given. It was said by the owners of the fish that they had brought it direct from Billingsgate Market on the same day, where stale and stinking fish had been substituted for the fish paid for. Attention was drawn to this in the monthly report to the Public Health and Housing Committee, which, through the medium of the press, indirectly drew the attention of the Fishmongers' Company and the Market Authorities, to a possible means by which such occurrences could be effectually dealt with. It is noticeable that a decline in the quantity of bad fish brought for condemnation has resulted. Retention of Body. On 10th August the body of a male child was found retained in a first floor front room used by two females as a living and sleeping room. The child was said to have died about seven days previously, and as extremely hot weather was prevailing at the time, the body was badly decomposed and odoriferous. In the absence of the Medical Officer of Health, Dr. Fearnley visited the premises and signed certificate for removal of body. The certificate was taken to one of His Majesty's Justices, who made an order for immediate removal and burial. The body was at once removed to the mortuary, and subsequently buried by the Guardians. Flooding. No flooding has occurred during the year in the low-lying portion of Hackney Wick, by which several houses and other premises in 183 the district have been formerly rendered temporarily unfit for habitation. Cow and Slaughter Houses. The cow and slaughter houses in the district have received attention throughout the year, and found generally satisfactory. Ice Cream. The shops wherein ice cream is made or sold have been visited periodically. A great improvement has taken place in the conditions under which the cream is made and stored. The itinerant vendors throughout the district have been visited from time to time, and found to comply with the new regulations. with two exceptions. On 19th April a street ice cream vendor (alien) was discovered with a barrow and stall, which did not bear name and address of person from whom ice cream was obtained. A summons was authorised by the Public Health Committee to be taken out. At Worship Street Police Court on 19th May, the said vendor was fined 5s. and 2s. costs. One other vendor (alien) was found to have his own name exhibited, and not that of the person from whom the ice cream was obtained. The proper name and address were affixed to barrow on the vendor's attention being drawn to the irregularity. Eating Houses and Coffee Shops. The whole of the eating houses and coffee shops within the district have been specially inspected and generally were found in a fairly good condition. 184 In one or two instances the kitchen required some lime-whiting, which was immediately done upon the service of intimation. County Council Sewers. The nuisance from open ventilators in roadways has been considerably reduced, but not entirely obviated, since the installation of the shaft ventilators has been completed. Food and Drugs Acts. Fifty-three varied samples of food have been obtained and submitted to the Public Analyst for analysis, of which five were certified to have been adulterated, in two instances convictions were obtained with costs, and in two instances vendors were cautioned, and one case was dismissed on a warranty. A sample of mustard was found on analysis to be adulterated with 5 per cent. of wheaten flour. The vendor was cautioned by instruction of the Public Health and Housing Committee. A sample of milk was found on analysis to be deficient in fat and solids not fat to the extent of 20 and 13.5 per cent. respectively. At the Thames Police Court the vendor was fined £3 and 23s. costs. A sample of milk proved on analysis to be deficient in fat and solids not fat to the extent of 15 and 5 per cent. respectively. The vendor, a company, relied with success upon the warranty of the farmer who supplied the milk. The magistrate was satisfied, after hearing the evidence, that the warranty was, in this case, an effective defence, and accordingly dismissed the summons, but allowed no costs against the Council. A sample of milk contained a proportion of a foreign ingredient: Formaldehyd, 0.01 per cent. A summons was issued, and at the hearing at Thames Police Court the defendant was fined 1s. and 3 guineas costs. A sample of butter was adulterated with 74.8 per cent. of foreign 185 fat. On the instructions of the Public Health and Housing Committee the vendor was cautioned by a letter from the Medical Officer of Health. Closing Orders. On 16th December, summonses, under the Housing of the Working Classes Acts, 1891, 1893, were heard at the Thames Police Court against the owner of Nos. 4, 5, 6, 7, 13, 14, Brewery Yard. After hearing the evidence the magistrate withheld his decision for 14 days, to enable him to visit the premises. At the adjourned hearing on 30th December, the magistrate made a closing order on each of the houses with 25s. costs in each case, and an allowance of 10s. compensation to the tenants of four of the houses and 15s. in respect of one of them. One unoccupied. The whole of the houses were eventually vacated. These were very old worn-out structures; two were entirely wooden, two partly wood and partly brickwork and two entirely brickwork. The exact age does not appear to be known, but they must have been very old, and were strongly marked by evidence of decay, and signs of an early dissolution. These were cottages which were included in a scheme represented to the London County Council under Part I. of the Act, which the County Council refused to entertain as a county scheme. Two of the cottages, Nos. 6 and 7, have been demolished since by the owner on the requisition of the District Surveyor. This is the first representation made with respect of the Bow District, and it is regrettable the County Council should not have passed a resolution that such area was an unhealthy area, and forthwith proceeded to make a scheme for the improvement of such area, particularly as these cottages were very badly designed and fitted, and the accommodation and general ariangement were such as to be a menace to all within their immediate neighbourhood. Had a fire occurred within one of them the whole of them and several others must have been immediately reduced to ashes, owing to the close contiguity which amounted to a continuity of one to the other. The area was also 186 overshadowed and adjoined by a large hall of public resort, the main exit door opening direct into Brewery Yard, on/ the other side was a block of tenement dwellings, the close proximity of which must have added anxiety to the Council Fire Brigade had a conflagration occurred. Result of Legal Proceedings. 238 Pepper Adulterated with 10 £ of Oatmeal Fined 1/- and 23/costs 247 Milk Deficient in fat to the extent of 16.7 % Fined 20/- and 12/6 costs 256 Milk Deficient in solids not fat and fat, to the extent of 27.5 and 17.7 J respectively Fined 20/- and 23/costs Milk Shops. There are 57 milk shops in the district and these were inspected periodically. On a few occasions the shop keeper had to be cautioned about the cleanliness of the milk vessels and utensils. The shop keepers are all duly registered by name. Cowhouses and Dairies. There are three cowhouses in the district and these are thoroughly inspected four times a year, to see that the cleansing is carried out. This is done without any trouble. Frequent inspections are also paid to see that the hands of the milkers are clean during the operation. A caution was necessary in one shed. Slaughter Houses. There are four licensed in the district. These are periodically inspected, and are kept in a satisfactory condition. 192 Eating Houses. These were inspected and found satisfactory. Only in two instances intimations were served respecting the cleansing; and the work was done forthwith. Markets and Stalls. Much attention has been paid to these with a view of preventing the sale of unsound food. Night inspections have also been made, and good results have been obtained. The class of meat sold, has, in my opinion, been sound. Unsound Food. Animal food has been closely inspected in this district, whether the same has come from the Central market or from the out-lying districts. Several pig's heads have been surrendered owing to being diseased, but the carcases belonging to the heads were not upon the premises, as the heads are bought separately. In a sausage factory some diseased lungs were found, seized, and taken before the magistrate to be condemned. A summons was taken out against the butcher and at the Thames Police Court he was fined £10 10s. and £5 5s. costs. Observations have been kept at all hours during the night upon suspected traders in unsound food, with some good results. Bakehouses. These are often inspected, but there has been little cause of complaint. They are now classified as Workshops or Domestic Workshops. 193 Smoke Nuisances. Although there have been several complaints as to the amount of smoke issuing from various shafts, there have been no proceedings taken, except in one case, and that was in respect to the engine " Southend," belonging to the London, Tilbury and Southend Railway Company. The proceedings were taken upon my. report by the London County Council, and the railway company was fined 60s. Offensive Matter. Two summonses were taken out at the Thames Police Court with respect of offensive matter being conveyed through the streets in an improper manner. In each case a fine of 23s. was inflicted with 20s. costs. I am, Sir, Your obedient Servant, H. J. LANGLEY, Assoc. San. Inst. Cert Sanitary Inspector Exam. Vendor fined for having served same in an unstamped wrapper, and 23s. costs. Eating Homes. I have, as directed by you, inspected the eating houses (coffee shops) in my district, seven in number, where necessary have served " Intimations," and in compliance the work has been carried out. In no case was there cause for complaint so far as food was concerned. Bakehouses. I have, as specially instructed, made the periodical inspections of the 14 bakehouses, and where required served " Intimations." statutory notices, under the Factory and Workshop Act, 1901, for the necessary work of cleansing, and also in some cases sanitary repairs to be carried out. Dairy and Milk Sellers. The cowshed at the rear of 27, Abbott Road, and all the milk purveyor's premises have been frequently inspected, and the register corrected. In the cases of those found to be non-registered through change of proprietorship the new occupiers had signed forms of application in compliance with your instructions, and their names have been duly inserted in the register. Constant attention had been give to the enforcement of the regulations relating to cleanliness of premises, utensils, etc., but in no case has an infringement been discovered. Smoke Nuisances. I have, where a nuisance had occurred from smoke emitted from any chimney, other than belonging to a private dwelling-house, viz.:—Factories, workshops, and bakehouses, either on complaint or from my own personal observation, served " Intimation " notices, etc., and the nuisances for the time being had been abated in compliance with same. Examination of Food. I have on several occasions, upon different tradesmen s applications, examined articles of food purchased by them at the markets, 198 and when found to be unsound, unwholesome, and unfit for food of man, witnessed the same destroyed, and have given certificates to that effect. In no instance was any article of food exposed for sale, or deposited for the sale of or preparation for sale, at the time of my visit, in such a condition as to justify seizure. Inspection of Canal Boats (Barges). I have, during the year, made 57 inspections of non-registered barges, with the result that no infringement of regulations had been discovered. Schools. By your direction the five public schools in my district have been frequently visited, due care at the beginning of the year having been taken regarding the water supplies. All of which, with two private schools (both since closed) had been found to be kept in fair sanitary condition. Ice Cream Makers and Vendors. As directed by you, all the premises where ice cream was prepared were inspected with the view of detecting any infringement of the London County Councils (General Powers) Act, 1902. The barrows were examined to see that they were legibly marked with the vendors' names, etc., and care taken that the ice cream and utensils were satisfactory. In only one case was an offence detected, which was met by the business being discontinued. Cowshed License. • One notice had been received for renewal of license. The premises, No. 27, Abbott Road, were again duly inspected, found to be in fair sanitary condition, no objection was raised to the application. I am, Sir, Your obedient Servant, RICHARD E. To the Medical Officer of Health. Sir, I beg to lay before you my fourth Annual Report of the sanitary work and other duties carried out under the different Acts in connection therewith, in the West Combined district, during the past 12 months, rendering as far as possible a detailed account of nuisances abated, inspections made, summonses applied for, etc. In investigating complaints and making inspections of all kinds, 1.376 houses or other premises have been visited, 323 of this number being on account of infectious diseases, and 133 being house-to-house visits. The number of re-inspections necessary to follow up the Statutory Notices, and to see the work through totalled 3,787. On account of the L.C.C. making Chicken Pox a notifiable disease, during some portion of the year the number of notifications were considerably swelled, the 323 mentioned also included those from the Poplar Union, and from the Sick Asylum.
